to

In article <6dku57$dpj$1...@heliodor.xara.net>, James David Chapman
<jc...@nospam.globalnet.co.uk.I.said.NO.spam> writes

>Sean Timarco Baggaley <stbag...@hotmail.com> wrote:
>
>>Pro-technology people are forever whining about how 100% pure ASM
>>(orwhatever) is so bloody important, but it's bullshit. It's the
>>*GAME *that's important. Code quality is secondary, since without a good game
>>for the coders to actually work on, all that effort it pointless.
> You can imagine any game you like, with all sorts of neat features,
>and gameplay, but without an engine, designed to run on a particular
>machine, you are just daydreaming.

But an engine on its own is nothing. Doom, without the gameplay, is just
a koderz demo.

And you don't *need* bleeding edge 100% assembly language to create a
decent game. None of the ones I own (aside from Descent 1) are even 3D,
let alone hardware accelerated. And you can do a tilemapped game on a
486 in 100% C and still have to frame-lock the damned thing to stop it
running too fast.


<TOTALLY IRRELEVANT RANT>

I'd like to see more emphasis on letting people who *hate* joysticks and
mice get into a game. Penalising keyboard uses is downright rude, since
many people can't stand the standard mice and joysticks that PCs come
with.

Quake forces you to use a joystick (and, ideally, a mouse *as well*)
which, as far as I -- and my siblings -- are concerned makes it way too
much hassle to play; certainly more than its worth. Descent, Doom and
Duke3D did not. That ID saw fit to force people to use ID's preferred
control method is, frankly, a classic example of poor design. User
interface is an integral part of the game design and it should be
*TRANSPARENT*. *NO ONE* has a right to dictate an arbitrary control
method just because *they* like it.

Descent still ranks as one of my favourite games since it allowed total
control via keyboard. Sure, there were a lot of controls, but nothing
like as many as in, say, a flight sim. Because of this, there's a whole
rake of games coming out that I will simply *never* touch. Ever. By
deliberately alienating keyboard users (and I'm sure a lot of one-time
Spectrum owners will agree with me on this), developers are only
shooting themselves in the foot.

</TOTALLY IRRELEVANT RANT>


> Doom didn't come about by people sitting around thinking about how
>neat it would be to frag people, it came about because someone worked
>out how to code the 'bloody' thing.

It's a 'techie' game. It's an engine game. But it *did* have some damned
good game design wrapped around it.

This is far more than can be said for Quake, which should have had
'Minimum requirements: 2 players / Recommended: High-bandwidth WAN with
plenty of servers and at least 16 players' written on the box. As a
single-player game design, it stinks. If they'd spent a bit of their
effort on the game design, Quake may well have been a classic. As it is,
it's merely a design of form over content. (Quake 2 may well be a better
game, but my rant, above, explains why I'll never bother with it.)


>>If I'm faced with a choice between spending a month optimising for a
>><10% increase or spending that same month getting the *gameplay* right,
>>I'd choose the latter every time.

> Phew. Every 10% increase in optimisation allows you to add more
>features to a game, thus improving the gameplay and commercial value.

Arbitrarily adding features will not automatically improve gameplay.
They have to be *designed* first. What you are proposing is the gaming
equivalent of 'bloatware' -- features for the sake of it.

Glenn's 'discovery' -- "Action Supercross" -- discussed in another
thread is a prime example of this. Arbitrarily making it 3D would make
it a *totally* different game, and improving the current graphics will
not change the gameplay in any way. This isn't to say that Action
Supercross cannot be improved, just that any such improvements need to
be carefully thought out and designed. Even making the physics modelling
more 'realistic' would fuck up the essential gameplay.

Even coding it in 100% pure assembly, would make absolutely no tangible
improvement -- it could even make it worse by ruining the 'feel' of the
game. Certainly, making it faster could well make it unplayable for many
potential buyers. But there is one, fairly major, flaw in the conceptual
design: the pricing.

(Yes, commercial considerations *are* a part of design; even the best
screenplays are created with a view to sales figures. After all, there's
no point coming up with an incredibly funny comedy like 'Father Ted'
(RIP) if nobody's ever likely to *see* it.)

*

Another prime example of this -- and one you may well bring up in
support of your own case -- is 'Tetris'. In particular: 'Tetris' on the
Nintendo Gameboy. The reason this was (and still is) such a successful
game is not merely the programming. The quality of the underlying
program (which was written in the Hitachi variation of Z80 assembly
language) is not the most important contributor; that role lies in the
hands of the design.

The difficulty curve is spot-on. The controls are simple and
uncluttered. The front-end design is clear and easy to follow; in fact,
it's almost ignored by most people and that is how it *should* be. Even
the two-player game is well thought out. Sound is also well-used;
providing useful cues for the player with aural feedback and rewards.

But it's important to remember that designer *must* include the features
of the hardware. A lot of Gameboy Tetris' success stems *directly* from
features of the hardware. It's simple, clear, easy to understand and
unintimidating -- a 'WalkMan' for the games industry. The buttons are
responsive and they're *digital*, not analogue -- an important point.
Networking is a piece of cake: just join two machines with a cable. No
installation; no network cards; no TCP/IP stacks or drivers.

Simple and intuitive.

And, contrary to the thrust of your argument, absolutely *NOTHING* to do
with what language was used to write the game.

Speed was simply not a factor; in fact, it had to allow for the high-
latency LCD used, so it coulnd't be made too fast.

And, when you consider what the machine is capable of, it's clear that
Tetris isn't a big enough game for the programmer(s) to have to worry
about squeezing down the size of the final program.

It could have been written in Modula-2 for all the difference it would
have made. Assembly was used, simply because it was the best tool for
the job. (If memory serves, it was also the *only* tool for the job;
Gameboy programmers didn't have any choice in the matter.)


>>It's a 'mark-up language' -- that's what the 'M' and 'L' in 'HTML' stand
>>for. Just because it ain't used for programming doesn't make it any less
>>a language.

>[So morse code with a bit of formatting is a language too?].

Noooo... read my words again. HTML is a *MARK-UP* language adapted from
SGML. It's an incredibly tiny subset of SGML, which, I can assure you,
is *fucking* scary stuff to look at; C++ has *nothing* on this. SGML is
a mark-up language that has been used in the printing industry for
years. It's not the same thing as Adobe's PostScript(tm), but serves a
similar function for some high-end typesetting tasks.

(It's somewhat closer in concept to the LaTEX/TEX languages used at
universities for creating maths textbooks with loads of weird and wacky
formulae and the like.)

Morse Code is an aural representation of the Roman Alphabet, much as
ASCII as a numeric one. It isn't formattable in any useful way as the
aural symbols are designed only to be combined into words. This process
is technically known as 'spelling'. It is not a language, merely a
representation of the components of a specific sub-set of languages.

I'd have thought this was common knowledge.

--
Sean Timarco Baggaley

<mailto:stim...@cix.co.uk>

E&OE

:: especially if the OS is doing disk caching.
:
:Are you sure you've got that the right way round? If you can decompress at
:20 instructions per byte, and you get 50% compression, you're beating even
:a UDMA-33 hard drive by a substantial factor on a P200.
:
:(load 8M : 2 seconds. Load 4M and decompress : 1 second to load, 0.4
:seconds to decompress)
:
:Tom

:
:
:

I guess it depends on the compression technique used. For simple RLE image
encoding, compressed data is considerably faster than uncompressed data. But
graphics formats like JPEG are much slower to decompress than, say, an
uncompressed BMP.

Kalrithian's profile photo

to
William H. Ivey wrote in message <6dlcir$r...@sjx-ixn8.ix.netcom.com>...
:There's no assembly in our game, most of it would transfer directly


:to another platform (assuming the platform has an available C++
:compiler), and it runs so fast we never even took our copies of
:MASM out of their boxes... (Which were bought in the first place
:because of lobbying by a must-have-asm fanatic. I once worked on some
:of his C code - he'd left a comment that it needed asm to speed it
:up. Reworking a couple of loops sped it up 600% (yes, six hundred
:percent).)
:
:On an earlier project, some of the original asm library routines
:were rewritten in C and ran faster.-Wm

Which just goes to show that assembly is not inherently faster than C/C++,
any more than using octal constants generates faster code than using decimal
constants. C++ compilers nowadays are better than *most* assembly
programmers, and I think most here would agree that writing C++ code takes
*much* less time than equivalent assembly. C++ code can be recompiled across
platforms and architectures (even different members of a CPU family) with
little or no hassle. Assembly, OTOH, must basically be rewritten for every
new target. C++ code originally written for the 80386 can be optimized for
the Pentium-II with a quick recompile; 386-optimized assembly, OTOH, must be
rewritten to avoid Pentium stalls and to take advantage of changed opcode
timings. And forget about moving Intel assembly code to another chip ... or
even to a different assembler.

I guess is A Good Thing(tm) if you're a DOS Koder(tm). (You even get to do

Know your compiler. If you're a good assembly programmer (I'm assuming you
are), you should look at disassembled code generated by your compiler. This
is *much* less time-consuming than writing it all yourself in assembly.

:Guess what, set rules and an imaginative humnan mind are never


:evenly matched. You can never perdict whether Gerry Kasparov will

:challenge Deep Blue again to win. And guess what, Compiler technology are

I seriously doubt that statement. A compiler is nothing more than a
sophisticated AI engine that uses a large set of rules to choose the
smallest/fastest instructions to resolve a particular problem. Given the
right set of rules, a compiler will beat a human coder hands-down ... and in
*much* less time. (There aren't many assembly programmers out there that are
the equivalent of Kasparov, either...)

: A Human mind still cannot be beaten when it compares itself to a


:pegboard of set rules. Maybe the compiler can step through and
:revise routines faster than a human can, but the human, with innovation,
:experience and a bit of time, will always ended up with cleaner code.

Really? And I suppose you think you can square ten-digit numbers or
integrate in your head faster than a Pentium? Humans are 'creative', yes,
but computers are much better at following rules.

Avery Lee wrote:


>"Kalrithian" <eem...@txdirect.net> wrote:
>>Avery Lee wrote in message <35001049...@news.ucsb.edu>...
>>:It is true that C compilers still don't generate code comparable to
>>:hand-optimized x86 assembly.
>>Really? Last time I checked, Watcom (and now Visual C++) was beating most
>>assembly programmers by a fair margin ...
>

>On a Pentium Pro or Pentium II, maybe. On a Pentium, I doubt it. Not a
>chance on MMX applications.
Agreed on the MMX part. Float is also an area where you can win a lot by
cleverly interleaving and better FPU stack usage.

>If there's a way to exploit the carry flag in
>the computations, the asm programmer will probably win unless the compiler
>can do so as part of an intrinsic function.
True again. One of the *very* few exceptions and is only useable in a inner
loop that's out of registers.


>Huh? No driver support in Win32? Gee, what a surprise. Now try and find
>a C compiler that will schedule all the shifts and LEAs for the formula
>Y=(54*R+183*G+19*B)/256 to process a 32-bit xRGB pixel to grayscale in 18
>cycles or less on a non-MMX Pentium. If you find one, I'll be impressed.
>
>VC++ 4.0, which I use, is an old compiler, but I don't think they've
>improved it enough since to surpass the 200%+ speed increases in AVI frame
>processing I get from rewriting choice routines in asm.
Well, I wrote it in a way that actually *describes* what the compiler has to
do. Written the way you did it above, you have to first load R,G,B into
variables. Anyway, my line of code was:

y=(54*((dwPixel>>16)&0xff)+183*((dwPixel>>8)&0xff)+19*(dwPixel&0xff))/256;

And VC++ 5.0 produced the code at the bottom. At a first glance, it is
*very* close to 18 cycles and perhaps even under. I didn't count them. It's
19 simple instructions, and many interleave well. If it's more than 18
cycles, it's probably in the 18..22 range. So you'd get *at most*, if you
were only running this code, a 25% speedup.

Seems pretty impressive to me. VC++ 5.0 is really quite capable when it's
about integer optimizations.

Bye,
Robert

00401073 mov ecx,dword ptr [esp+8]
00401077 add esp,8
0040107A mov eax,ecx
0040107C and eax,0FFh
00401081 lea edx,dword ptr [eax+eax*8]
00401084 lea edx,dword ptr [eax+edx*2]
00401087 xor eax,eax
00401089 mov al,ch
0040108B lea ecx,dword ptr [eax+eax*2]
0040108E lea ecx,dword ptr [ecx+ecx*4]
00401091 lea eax,dword ptr [eax+ecx*4]
00401094 lea eax,dword ptr [eax+eax*2]
00401097 add edx,eax
00401099 xor eax,eax
0040109B mov al,byte ptr [esp+2]
0040109F lea eax,dword ptr [eax+eax*2]
004010A2 lea ecx,dword ptr [eax+eax*8]
004010A5 lea edx,dword ptr [edx+ecx*2]
004010A8 shr edx,8

In article <Pine.GSO.3.96.980304161628.3663C-
100...@xena.acsu.buffalo.edu>, Kevin Kwan <kk...@acsu.buffalo.edu>
writes
>Well, I agree with you totally.
I suppose somebody has to. Doesn't make either of you right, though.

> It is very easy for a programmer to be
>veiled by the "relative ease" of programming in Common SDKs and Midlevel
~~~~~~~~~~~~~
As opposed to the "utter impossibility" of performing the same tasks in
DOS? Let's go through this one last time: Most new hardware accelerators
are *ONLY* accessible through APIs. Since custom APIs are *EXCLUSIVE*,
it makes far more sense to go for a standardised API. Riva128 cards
*ONLY* work through APIs -- there are *NO* hardware specs available for
them. The folks behind the PowerVR chips are also moving towards
releasing drivers.

Nobody's claiming life is perfect, but forcing technology to remain
static solely because it makes *your* life easier is not the answer.
Remember, 90% of computer owners happen to be *CORPORATIONS*. They don't
buy their PCs to play games on. Games players make up only a tiny number
of PC sales, so statistics like these...

>And despite all the gaming magazine's
>suggestions that everybody have a 3dfx and a P2, guess what, 65% of the
>home computers today are still 486s and Vanilla Pentiums, and the number
>is even higher in Asia and Europe.

... are irrelevant, since 90% of those PCs are owned by big businesses.
A 486 DX2/66 is plenty fast enough to run many low-level applications --
I even had Win95 and Office 95 running happily on my one (until the
monitor died, sending 20000 volts through the graphics card).

For basic secretarial tasks, 486s and low-end Pentiums are more than
adequate, and many of these are simply migrated down from other
departments as they upgrade. The main reasons for upgrading at this
level, for a lot of corporates, are simple wear and tear, and the
diminishing returns of maintenance. (After a certain period, it becomes
almost impossible to find spares; like MCA cards for old PS/2 machines
and VESA/LB video cards).

Of that remaining 10% of PC buyers, most are used for SOHO work (a
rapidly expanding market sector). Others are bought for 'real' family
use (my sister and brothers have an Apple Mac LC III for their
school/university work. It's ancient, but it runs ClarisWorks without
any hiccups.)

At best, only about 1% of total PC sales can be attributed to the
hardcore gamers.

All those advances in technology -- and the continual upgrading -- is
*directly* attributable to mainstream application users. Desktop
publishing -- which has brought a lot of media work in-house -- becomes
more economical when done on faster computers. Creating and/or sourcing
graphics for publications, packaging design and even on-line media and
PR departments is common in large businesses. Images used for
publication are often tens of megabytes in size, so faster CPUs, more
memory and bigger hard drives are very popular for these tasks. Images
can be scanned; drawn by hand; created on 3D renderers...

Even those ludicrously expensive 3D packages we use in the games
industry are generally extensions to those used by other industries for
product design. (AutoCAD begat 3D Studio, for instance.)


>the code written by any compiler, no matter how good or how new it is,
>frankly sucks when you compare it to hand optimized assembly code.

This isn't the case with pro-quality compilers. Of course, if your
experiences are based on DJGPP/GNU and similar quality compilers, then
your reasoning makes more sense, but MS' Visual C++ 5 and Watcom's
compilers are to gcc what Rolls-Royce is to Skoda. Sure, they cost more,
but you get what you pay for.


>And in an industry in which game engine is fundemental,

I've answered this fallacy (or should that be 'phallusy'? :) in a
separate post. In brief: *game design* is fundamental; the game engine
is secondary to that. Games programming is merely the means to an end.
It is not an end in itself.

[...]
>(You have NO idea how C++ compilers actually did a bad job in
> optimizing Pentium code, sometimes causing Pipeline stalls for the
>sake of utilizing the V-Pipe in a couple of MOV instructions...and in
>the Superscalar Pentium, that's a major headache)

Actually, we do. Who do you think you're arguing with? Newbies? Uh-uh.
Unlike you, however, we don't tend to tar every compiler with the same
brush: those fuck-ups you mention only happen on *shite* compilers. Why
do you think development suites like Visual C++ and Watcom/Optima cost
so much?

What do you think all those coders of yesteryear are *doing* these days?
That's right! Writing games! When you talk about how coders squeezing
great games into 32K of RAM, don't forget that some of us *WERE* those
coders. Glenn Corpes (of Bullfrog), for instance, used to write in "100%
pure ASM(tm)" on Atari's ST and Commodore's Amiga. I started out on the
Sinclair Spectrum (and also did some Atari stuff), and there are other
veterans posting here. The reason we don't do it any more is because it
just isn't necessary. Even the lowest, cheapest, shoddiest second-hand
8Mb P60 PC for sale at £150.00 at the local computer fair is more than
capable of running PacMan while multi-tasking with MS Office in Win95.

And yes, current games *are* more complex. Command & Conquer is orders
of magnitude more complex than, say, Rebelstar Raiders or Laser Squad.
This increased complexity puts a lot of people off, especially those who
enjoyed those older, simpler, more immediate games. But the depth of
more recent games certainly appeals to others. It's a trend -- a fashion
-- and as such, it will change. (Remember the flood of puzzle games that
got released to fight over the Tetris market?)


>game like iF-16 to choke on my computer, while the graphically superior
>Novalogic F-22 Lightning can run better on a computer that is 35%
>slower.

This is to do with programming *quality*. The choice of overall language
is irrelevant, since 99% of the game will be spent in only 1% of the
code. As long as that 1% of code is optimal (and you *can* optimise C
and C++ if you know what you're doing), that 1% will run damned fast. As
someone else pointed out in this thread: it's the algorithm that's
slowing things down. Get the algorithm spot-on and you're made.


I'm surprised by your choice of a NovaLogic game to illustrate your
argument. Firstly, they like using Voxel Space instead of polygonal 3D,
so their engine isn't even remotely comparable. Voxel space tech isn't
particularly flexible, as you can see from NovaLogic's output since they
released Comanche.

Novalogic are one of the few companies still insisting on 100% pure
assembly for their game engines, but you'll notice that they seem to be
following a rather predictable pattern: "Flight Sim -> Tank Sim -> New
Iteration of Engine -> Flight Sim -> Tank Sim ... etc." Hardly
innovative or original games. What's more, NovaLogic are taking a damned
long time between each release... coincidence? I think not.

Bullfrog, on the other hand, seem to churn out original game after
original game (barring the occasional sequel). And they're averaging at
least one product a year. Sadly, NovaLogic's games all look the same
and, er, play very similarly. Extreme Assault (BlueByte) was a far
better game -- and it runs great even on my own, pre-MMX, unaccelerated
P166.


> Of course, ultimately, many users will get so fed up with the
>lack of feeling of responsiveness on a game even with its super-duper
>features on the fastest possible machine that either they'll jump ship
>to console games (The mass migration to the playstation, which have
>roughly the firepower of a P90 with a Virge VX card and some zippy DSPs
>attached, already made some PC games manufacturers uncomfortable), play
>older and more proven games (Emulators, games from 1994-5) or are
>forced to buy games with less features (or don't enable them) to play
>them on their PCs.

Er, what makes you think that a feeling of responsiveness is the be-all
and end-all of a game? Myst isn't exactly a hi-octane shooter, is it?
Did rather better than Quake, though. (And, if memory serves, it was
written in Macromedia's Director package.)

As for PlayStation: you do know that programs are written for it *IN C*!
Granted, it's not *great* C, since it's only a cacky GNU compiler, but
that's not important: the hardware does all the work! That's why we
*need* hardware acceleration on the PC: it frees up the CPU (and the
programmers) from continually re-inventing the same damned wheel.

Why should flight sim programmers have to keep writing new 3D engines?
Why not just use a scalable engine 'off the shelf' (like D3D, OpenGL or
Fahrenheit) and concentrate on making a great game?

By leaving the development of the 3D engine in the hands of dedicated
specialists, you can simply write your game to that and it will -- if
written *properly* -- automatically take advantage of newer, improved
hardware. This will also have the side-effect of allowing future owners
of 'legacy' PCs (with "ancient Voodoo 1 chipsets and a mere 32Mb RAM!")
to last longer, since you won't have to keep swapping out CPUs and stuff
all the time: just upgrade to the next version of the graphics card. It
won't cure the Moore's Law Effect entirely, but it should alleviate it
by shifting the emphasis from CPUs to peripherals.

>Anyway, what I'm saying is, despite the propaganda Intel continues to toss
>around saying that you're less than human if you don't have a Pentium, and
>then you're pond scum unless you have MMX, and you're a dung beetle if
>your PC is not a P2, when you look at the way the PC is organized, the CPU
>actually spend a lot of time waiting for other components to respond, and
>the CPU still are not as powerful as some programmers would like,

Speak for yourself. If I want fast 3D, I'll buy a 3D accelerator -- much
cheaper than buying a faster Pentium (or ripping out the motherboard and
getting a PII). Then I'll just call a suitable API and have done with
it. It's only fucking display code, after all. It's not like we're
talking about brand-new, earthshattering advances in programming
technology.

Technically, Quake doesn't even do anything original -- all of those
techniques can be got out of books like Screen Gems and their ilk. The
only reason Quake can be done today is because we have the computing
power and hardware to handle it. You can't do 256-colour, light-sourced,
gouraud-shaded texture-mapped polygons on a Commodore C64 because it
cannot even *display* 256 colours. (And with only a 1Mhz 8-bit CPU,
you'd be waiting all day for it to render a single frame.)


> and in certain critical routines, You'll still have to drop to NASM to
>do a little creative Assembly to code certain routinesto make them play
>better.

NASM? If your compiler experience consists of DJGPP, it's not surprising
you've got such a dim view of computer-assisted optimisation. ('CAO'!
New Labour: New Acronym!)

DJGPP is fine for hobbyist work, but it's waaaayyyy behind the times.
It's simply nowhere near as good as the likes of Watcom, Borland and
Microsoft's C/C++ compilers. (If you're a student, you can get a major
discount on some of these, BTW.)


>Often, the newest features, the newest technology and the best graphics
>does not automatically guarantee sales, It's gameplay and editability.

"Editability"? Do all games players have to want to do the level
designer's job for them? When I buy a game, I don't expect to have to
create my own damned levels as well -- not for *that* sort of money.
(ID, please take note.)


>Who cares about the 16 bit bitmapping, the super-duper realtime
>campaign engine, the superduper AI, or thousand polygon bitmapped
>meshes if it only runs at 9 fps on an average Joe's PC, even if the

Eh? You can't *have* 16-bit mipmaps if you're running in "I've got a
shite PC" mode. At least, not on the games I've played. Exactly how
would you get 65000+ colours on a 256 colour palettised display?

The simple fact is that many complainants simply don't *want* to turn
off those features. Unfortunately, there's not a lot anyone can do.
Quake 2 simply isn't possible on a 386.

This is *exactly* the same attitude that killed off the Commodore Amiga
and Atari ST. (In fact, it was the Atari Falcon that snapped *me* out of
it; my platform game ran at 72fps! Faster than Sonic! Played better,
too. But by then, PCs were beginning to come into their own so I jumped
straight over to those.)


>Anyway, I totally agree that Assembly should be an option in the arsenal

Jason Shankel's profile photo
Jason Shankel
unread,
Mar 5, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to
Ah, gotcha. I misunderstood you.
Anyway, this thread is about to break down into a religious flame fest
(just like it always does). But before we completely lose sight of the
topic, I'd like to answer the original question.

The original question, which was fairly well put, was "Why isn't ASM the
game language of choice?"

The answer, ultimately, is that the cost/benefit analysis doesn't play
out in ASM's favor. For the majority of code in a project, cost of
spending extra months of development time and sacrificing portability
and maintainability do not justify the benefit of a small speed increase
and a small size decrease.

For a critical, crucial, sine qua non, no getting around it, without it
you have no game minority of the code, the benefit of even a slight
speed increase is often worth the cost of using ASM. In these cases,
still, you write your first version in C and you keep your optimized ASM
algorithms up to date in C code, for portability and readability.

As professionals, we cannot afford to be language bigots or to entrench
ourselves too firmly in religiousity. You need to be able to identify
the right tool for the job and to know when to apply those tools.

Code size and execution speed are always important, but they are not the
only priorities.


--
Jason Shankel,
Maxis, Inc.

>Therefore, Doom probably did start with an engine. An engine
>which is about 5% asm, BTW...


Doom was far less than 5% asm. I used to work at Papyrus, and Papyrus was
considered (at one point) for porting Doom to the Macintosh. According to an
email from John Carmack, there were 3 assembly language functions:

horizontal texture map
vertical stretch
a joystick reading routine

The rest of Doom was in C. Check out the Doom sources at ftp.idsoftware.com.

time
(all variables on the stack for example) and it was worth saving that
one
off 50k hit for using C when you only had 512k of RAM on an ST/Amiga,

than basic (you would have loved it back in the early eighties).

the largest code (not executable) size i've ever seen on a game is about
200k, if C wasted 50% of that memory (it doesn't, most of it wastes
nothing) the most you would save is 100k, that's 1/20th of what you are
talking about.

>
>
> >Execution speed for outer loop functions is less of a critical issue.
>
> I am happy to agree with this again.

>
> >But high level languages provide adequate speed for anything which
> >doesn't have to be rip-roaring fast.

> Init routines have been mentioned, menus, maybe, if they are not
> displayed by the game engine itself, everything else is inside some
> kind of loop, so you'd better be careful.

How about some 'typical' game code like this, I know exactly (with the
exception of register usage) what a good C compiler will do with this.

struct Missile
{
int x;
int y;
int vx;
int vy;
};

Missile missiles[NO_MISSILES];

move_missiles()
{
Missile* misptr = missiles;
for(count = NO_MISSILES; count>0; misptr++, count--)
{
misptr->x += misptr->vx;
misptr->y += misptr->vy;
colide_check(misptr);
}
}

How would you do this in assembler?, no changing the algorithm BTW, I
know
it's shit.

> Surely this attitude you have: "It doesn't matter how large the exe
> is/almost all the code is non-time crucial", is linked to the reason
> why people have to upgrade their computers every year? Surely you can
> see that? :)

No, that's linked to the fact that texture mappers use between five and
eight instructions per pixel while sprite draws often used less than
one,
that 640*480 has 4.8 times the pixels of 320*200, that 3D games are
using
10 times the geometry...

>
> And surely you can see that, from my point of view, the number of
> routines which are 'allowed' to be large and slow is increasing every

Not large and not slow, unless the programmer is using C as if it was
basic, maybe some do, it's not C's fault.

> >What? And spent months patching a game that runs too

> You seem to have completely misunderstood. If you're
> writing a game for a target of a P166, and it runs well
> on a P166, that's great. If you then start rewriting it in
> asm to get it working on a P133, then you are wasting
> time and money from the publisher's view. They are
> going to get about 1% of sales on P133s so they are
> not going to make the extra development costs back.
Oh yeah? Since when does a game that run ever run good on a target
machine, anyway? Often, the statement is:
You CAN run it on a P133, but you're better off on a 166.
The CAN part attracts 133 customers, who return home to run it, discover
it don't work well, can't return it, and then ended up bitching about it
to half of usenet. That's one reason why people go into warez! If they
d/l a game that don't run well, all they do is delete it, and then
bitch...No loss to him or her except for time on the d/l. And if they
like it, chances are, he or she will buy it for the cutscenes, music or
effects that was patched out by the release group.
Things like these create negative publicity, which persists until you
conclusively proves that you can make a better product the next timne
around.
What programmers can do is at least post a higher requirement, which at
least is a sign of integrity, then to post a lower requirement with the
pre-requisite that the user deactivate many of the features that make the
user interested in the first place, which is a lower form of defrauding
the consumer.

> Ports cost money. id isn't making a profit on Linux Quake.
> They did it, at a loss, because they felt like it. Who gives
> a damn about cornering a market that's too small to even
> have shop space devoted to it? FFS, my local EB still has
> a rack of Amiga games!

Yeah, But doing so brings respect and consumer confidence, which counts in
the long run. The short term goal of paying the bills don't matter if
your product corners the market in the intended platform anyway.

> >At least id is smart enough to reach out to the 8%
>
> 8%? Linux has about 2% of the Pentium-class PC market

> doubt that I'm *two* of those 'users' that Linux advocates
> like to quote...

It isn't. But it is 8% of the total PC market (Market segmentation isd
important) of Ps, P2s, PPros and 486s. And the number of people who uses
Linux and any UNIX clones continue to grow, as NT and Windows 95 cannot
handle all the scalability, performance and price Unix clones can
currently offer. That number tends to grow as people switch from M$ to
UNIXes to "Piss Bill Gates off", and even if only they experiement with
Dual Booting Windows 95/Linux for reasons of practicality.
It is true that Linux is not a consumer OS...It's more of an academic OS,
what hackers use to "learn the system". But still, people who uses Linux
tend to be the most active and intelligent Quake developers out there, and
since id earned their respect and loyalty with the Quake port, I'm sure id
won a major battle here. Just check out Jedi Knights, which is better
than Quake/Quake 2 in every way? How many Quake and Quake 2 developers
have you heard of compared to Jedi Knights? And with Quake, the more
modifications there is, the more sales id rack in.

to

ule...@mcl.ucsb.edu said:
> "Kalrithian" <eem...@txdirect.net> wrote:
> >Avery Lee wrote:
> >:It is true that C compilers still don't generate code comparable to
> >:hand-optimized x86 assembly.
> >Really? Last time I checked, Watcom (and now Visual C++) was beating most
> >assembly programmers by a fair margin ...
Actually, the question I have is, how have you characterized "most assembly
programmers"? Do you know a fair cross section of us? The assembler
programmers I can think of include Jorn Nystad, Terje Mathisen, Damien
Jones, John Fine, Andrew Howe, Dave Stafford, Matt Pritchard, John
Erckerdal, Michael Abrash, Agner Fog, Karki Jitendra Bahadur, Vesa Karvonen,
Jim Neil, Tennie Remmel, Phil Carmody etc. I don't think WATCOM would beat
many of these guys on any piece of code.

> On a Pentium Pro or Pentium II, maybe.

That's just because there isn't a "P6 optimization guide". :o) (I'm
looking into it though ...)

> [...] On a Pentium, I doubt it. Not a chance on MMX applications. If
> there's a way to exploit the carry flag in the computations, the asm
> programmer will probably win unless the compiler can do so as part of an
> intrinsic function.

Indeed, the carry flag is the friend of 8088, ..., P55C. You will find,
however, that the K6 really hates using the carry flag.

> >:Not even close, especially in audio and video processing.
> >Of course, those of us who moved to Windows don't need to fret about this
> >kind of thing anymore. [Please refrain from starting a DOS vs. Windows
> >flamewar over the previous statement. I apologize in advance for any injured
> >egos.]
>
> Windows will not do everything for you. Try to find hardware acceleration
> for sharpening or converting images to grayscale.
>
> Huh? No driver support in Win32? Gee, what a surprise. Now try and find
> a C compiler that will schedule all the shifts and LEAs for the formula
> Y=(54*R+183*G+19*B)/256 to process a 32-bit xRGB pixel to grayscale in 18
> cycles or less on a non-MMX Pentium. If you find one, I'll be impressed.

Well, I agree that you aren't going to find a C compiler that does that, but
you can beat 18 cycles fairly easily:

lea edi,[ecx+ecx*8] ; U edi = B * 9
lea eax,[eax+eax*2] ; V eax = R * 3
lea edx,[ebx+ebx*8] ; U edx = G * 9
shl eax,1 ; V eax = R * 6
lea ecx,[ecx+edi*2] ; U ecx = B * 19
lea ebx,[ebx+ebx*2] ; V ebx = G * 3
lea eax,[eax+eax*8] ; 1 eax = R * 54
shl ebx,06H ; U ebx = G * 192
add eax,ecx ; V eax = R * 54 + B * 19
sub ebx,edx ; 1 ebx = G * 183
add eax,ebx ; 1 eax = R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19
shr eax,8 ; 1 eax =(R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19)/256

8 cycles on a Pentium, n'est-ce pas (plus overhead)? You can of course,
look up "optimal multiplies" at:

http://www.pobox.com/~qed/amult.html

--
Paul Hsieh
email,finger: q...@pobox.com
URL: http://www.pobox.com/~qed/asm.html

to

James David Chapman wrote:
>
> > Assembler code you so lovingly craft at the beginning of a project is likely to be sub-optimal
> >on the hardware which exists when you ship.
>
> I really don't find this. Routines just run faster and faster.
You're telling me that your highly optimal 486 code is also highly
optimal on the Pentium? That your highly optimal non-MMX code is also
highly optimal on an MMX machine? Come on.

> >As for the "I'm tired of being forced to upgrade every year" thing...I
> >simpathize, believe me.
>
> If you did more than just simpathize, and actually worked *with*
> machine coders to speed up your programs, then you could actually
> help the situation...

Who says I don't?
Read My Lips: I use assembly to optimize the speed-critical sections of
my code. Optimizing the non-speed critical sections of my code will not
reduce the minimum platform requirement.

> >If your title runs well on a low-end
> >machine, that's an indication that you are not "cutting edge".
>
> This is desperately sad. I have yet to program a game which cannot
> eat up all the speed available to me on today's computers.

But can you code the same app to run well on yesterday's machines?
That's my point.

Is it "desperately sad" that Quake II does not run well on a 486?
They're the ones who are outcompeting the rest of us. It's not just
about color depth and video mode. When you're talking about a low-end
machine being 1/2 the speed of a high-end machine (a gap which gets
wider in real terms every year), then you really do have to pick a zone
of support.

> >Sorry for the rant, but you asked why ASM has fallen out of favor. ASM,
> >in my experience, is certainly not dead. It is just not as universally
> >appropriate as it used to be. That does not diminish its importance.
>
> If what you said was entirely correct, of course it would diminish
> its importance. If you really could write an OS in C, or as fast a
> game, as a machine is able to run, then asm would be stuffed into
> history.

Again, I didn't SAY THAT. I said that ASM is not as universally
applicable as it used to be. Something can still be totally critical
even if it's applicability is diminished.

Again: ASM is still an important factor in optimizing speed critical
elements of code.

There is a BIG difference between that and claiming that 100% of a title
should be developed in assembly. A BIG difference.

I repeat: ASM is an important factor in optimizing speed critical
code. It would be very difficult to optmize speed critical code without
at least knowledge of assembly. Without assembly, I would not be able
to optimize my speed critical code. I routinely optmizie critical code
in assembly. Assembly is the appropriate language for the optimization
of speed critical code.

That being said, for various reasons having to do with the realities of
professional software engineering, C, C++ and other high-level languages
are appropriate choices for developing the non-speed, non-size critical
elements of an application.
>
> You do seem to recognise the some of the importance of asm, but you
> say you are speaking for a few people here. I am not so sure, from my
> lurking, I have noticed an unhealthy disregard for asm in this group..
> hence my attempts to redress the balance.

I speak only for myself, but I am expressing sentiments I have
encountered elsewhere. Some developers deeply dislike getting their
hands dirty with asm. Others agree with your basic position. I have
found that the more successful developers understand all the tools at
their disposal and pick the appropriate tool for the job. As much as
possible, I try to place myself in the third camp.

DOOM was written mostly in C.
>
> Phew. Every 10% increase in optimisation allows you to add more
> features to a game, thus improving the gameplay and commercial value.

Sure, every 10%. But you only get one 10% (if we agree on the number,
depends on the title) by going to assembly. You don't get a 10%
increase every time you do an assembly pass.

Sean Timarco Baggaley wrote:
>
>
> Really? Ever played some of those 8-bit games?
>

Played many of them. But the point that I was making not that older
games and software was bug free, but rather is was written tighter
because of resource limitations. For example, to day most programers
don't think twice about allocating whole bytes ( or more) for boolean
values, where in days past programmers would group boolean values into
one byte. Result: less space and a tighter program.

Of course it was that same style programming that begot us the y2k
problem. I can just picture in my mind some COBOL programmers from the
60's going something like this:

Programmer #1: Damn, this programs data base is huge. They'll fill
the HDA's in no time.

Programmer #2: Have you optimized the data yet?

Programmer #1: Yes, but its still big.

Programmer #2: There must be something else we can do. Hey, I got
it! Have you ever noticed how your checks have the '19' pre-printed on
them?

Programmer #1: Yes, so what?

Programmer #2: So, you don't have to write the '19' because you'll
get more checks before the next century. If you leave out the 19 from
the year you'll save 2 bytes per record, That's 2k per 1000's records.
Were saved.

Programmer #1: Yes, but its only good for 40's years or so.

Programmer #2: Fuck it. It's only going to be in service for a
couple years and who ever redoes it can fix this.



Paul Hsieh's profile photo
Paul Hsieh
unread,
Mar 5, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

q...@chromatic.com said:
> > [...] Now try and find

> > a C compiler that will schedule all the shifts and LEAs for the formula
> > Y=(54*R+183*G+19*B)/256 to process a 32-bit xRGB pixel to grayscale in 18
> > cycles or less on a non-MMX Pentium. If you find one, I'll be impressed.
>
> Well, I agree that you aren't going to find a C compiler that does that, but
> you can beat 18 cycles fairly easily:
>
> lea edi,[ecx+ecx*8] ; U edi = B * 9
> [... etc deleted]
Doh! Don't listen to any of that. Its incorrectly paired and miscommented.
Lets try the following instead:

LoopTop:
mov ecx,[esi+ebp] ; U ecx = ?.R.G.B
xor eax,eax ; V eax = 0

mov bl,ch ; U ebx = G
mov al,[esi+ebp+2] ; V eax = R

and ecx,0ffh ; U ecx = B
add esi,4 ; V esi++

lea edi,[ecx+ecx*8] ; U edi = B * 9
lea eax,[eax+eax*2] ; V eax = R * 3

lea edx,[ebx+ebx*8] ; U edx = G * 9

add eax,eax ; V eax = R * 6

lea ebx,[ebx+ebx*2] ; U ebx = G * 3
lea ecx,[ecx+edi*2] ; V ecx = B * 19

shl ebx,06H ; U ebx = G * 192

lea eax,[eax+eax*8] ; V eax = R * 54

sub ebx,edx ; U ebx = G * 183


add eax,ecx ; V eax = R * 54 + B * 19

add eax,ebx ; U eax = R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19
xor ebx,ebx ; V ebx = 0

shr eax,8 ; U eax =(R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19)/256
cmp esi,[Limit] ; V+1 ZF = (esi==*Limit)

mov [esi],al ; U store
jnz LoopTop ; V branch

; 13 clocks per iteration on a P54/P55. (Similar pairing, but 12 clocks on
; an AMD K6 since branch target takes 0 clocks.)

LoopTop:
mov ecx,[esi+ebp] ; Ld ecx = ?.R.G.B
xor eax,eax ; Alu eax = 0 (anti-PRS)
mov bl,ch ; Alu ebx = G (bypass)

mov al,[esi+ebp+2] ; Ld eax = R
and ecx,0ffh ; Alu ecx = B
add esi,4 ; Alu esi++

lea edi,[ecx+ecx*8] ; St edi = B * 9

lea eax,[eax+eax*2] ; St eax = R * 3

lea edx,[ebx+ebx*8] ; St edx = G * 9
add eax,eax ; Alu eax = R * 6

lea ebx,[ebx+ebx*2] ; St ebx = G * 3

lea ecx,[ecx+edi*2] ; St ecx = B * 19
shl ebx,06H ; Alu ebx = G * 192

lea eax,[eax+eax*8] ; St eax = R * 54
sub ebx,edx ; Alu ebx = G * 183 (+1 Ex)

add eax,ecx ; Alu eax = R * 54 + B * 19

add eax,ebx ; Alu eax = R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19

xor ebx,ebx ; Alu ebx = 0
shr eax,8 ; Alu eax =(R * 54 + G * 183 + B * 19)/256
cmp esi,[Limit] ; Ld ZF = (esi==*Limit)

; Alu
mov [esi],al ; Sto store
jnz LoopTop ; Bra branch

; Hmm ... looks like 12 clocks for a P6.

--
Paul Hsieh

URL: http://www.pobox.com/~qed/amult.html

> shl eax,1 ; V eax = R * 6
> lea ecx,[ecx+edi*2] ; U ecx = B * 19
> lea ebx,[ebx+ebx*2] ; V ebx = G * 3
> lea eax,[eax+eax*8] ; 1 eax = R * 54

Oui, c'est bon. The overhead (assuming you mean the splitting
of the xRGB dword) is likely to be significant, though.

I'd go for 4k of tables (256 ints for R,G,B and Grey). I think that
manages under 12 clocks (assuming cache hits) to convert one
xRGB dword to another. I'll code it up in C tomorrow and see
what MSVC can make of it. I'd bet it's well under 18 clocks...

With the same inputs/outputs as above, I'd guess something like:
(1u) mov eax,[RedTable+eax*4]
(1v) mov ebx,[GreenTable+ebx*4]
(2u) mov ecx,[BlueTable+ecx*4]
(2v) add eax,ebx
(3u) add eax,ecx
(4u) shr eax,8
would do fairly well in a tight loop (i.e.: turning a whole image into
greyscale, rather than a single pixel).

Sean Timarco Baggaley wrote:
> What do you think all those coders of yesteryear are *doing* these days?
> That's right! Writing games! When you talk about how coders squeezing
> great games into 32K of RAM, don't forget that some of us *WERE* those
> coders. Glenn Corpes (of Bullfrog), for instance, used to write in "100%
> pure ASM(tm)" on Atari's ST and Commodore's Amiga. I started out on the
> Sinclair Spectrum (and also did some Atari stuff), and there are other
> veterans posting here.

I'll never love another machine the way I loved the Spectrum...

Yeah, time for me to stick my head up and agree. Back when I started
programming professionaly (early '80s) I was one of those 100% assembly,
obsessively optimising, loves to work on the bare metal programmers that
James (James? Whoever started the thread anyway...) talks about. At that
time, of course, there was no other way to do games. I spent five years
writing assembly everyday for the Spectrum (yeah!), the Amstrad, and the
original Nintendo. I spent weeks optimising software sprite routines in
strange and evil ways. You could have slapped me awake at four a.m.,
shone a flashlight in my face and asked me the number of clock cycles
taken by any Z-80 instruction and I would have known it.

And you know what? I am perfectly delighted that those days are over.
Optimising assembly offers some intellectual challenges. but so does
every other form of programming. The only difference is that using
higher level tools on more powerful platforms means that you can
accomplish more interesting things in the same time.

As we all agree, assembly has its place in particular games in
particular circumstances. So use the tool for the job, sure. My rule of
thumb though is to always use the most powerful and convenient tool
which will do the job as well as it needs to be done.

C is good. C++ is good. VB, Delphi, Director, Hypercard - they're good
too in their own ways, because they all do what a programming tool is
suppossed to do - allow you to get your work done without martyring
yourself.

> Speak for yourself. If I want fast 3D, I'll buy a 3D accelerator -- much
> cheaper than buying a faster Pentium (or ripping out the motherboard and
> getting a PII). Then I'll just call a suitable API and have done with
> it. It's only fucking display code, after all.

Hell, I've just started using a version of Smalltalk which has DirectX
bindings. *That's* what I call riding in luxury.

> Sean Timarco Baggaley

Sean Timarco Baggaley's profile photo
Sean Timarco Baggaley
unread,
Mar 6, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

In article <34FF5B69...@mindspring.com>, Pat Smith
<quan...@mindspring.com> writes
>Sean Timarco Baggaley wrote:
>> Really? Ever played some of those 8-bit games?

>Played many of them. But the point that I was making not that older
>games and software was bug free, but rather is was written tighter
>because of resource limitations.
> For example, to day most programers
>don't think twice about allocating whole bytes ( or more) for boolean
>values, where in days past programmers would group boolean values into
>one byte. Result: less space and a tighter program.

True, but 'tighter' != 'faster' or 'better'.

(To be fair, I've seen the source code of a #1 game that used 'int's (32
bits in Watcom C) to store a single flag. But I don't think anyone would
claim that it was well-written.)

The fact is that tight *data* was the necessity; the code was still a
fairly small proportion of a game, even back then. Most 8-bit coders
spent a depressing amount of program time and effort deciding whether to
compress data in some way (less space), weighing it against the down-
side of having to write extraction code for it (longer and slower).

Using bit-flags in 8-bit games was common sense at the time, but on
8-bit CPUs, it wasn't particularly fast. Bit operations often took a
fair number of cycles (which had to be taken into account since speed
was often important -- especially on non-hardware-assisted computers).
Even worse, the early CPUs (Z80, 6502/6510, 68000) didn't have luxuries
we take for granted today, such as barrel-shifters. Rotating bits into a
carry flag on a 68000 ate additional cycles for *each* bit rotated.

The data compression tended to slow things down a lot. The design had to
be optimised to allow data to be decompressed and accessed as quickly as
possible, since *all* of a level's data had to be resident in the
computer's memory. No cacheing of sprites or delayed loading of backdrop
data was possible back then, as the preferred storage media was cassette
tape. (Disk drives were more popular in the US, but the Commodore 64's
infamous "Hippo" was hardly a speed demon.)

The code:data ratio remained very similar for 8-bit machines right up to
today. This is because, the data being shunted around was much smaller
back then. Spectrums only had a resolution of 256x192... in monochrome.
Colour was overlaid using a separate attribute map with only 2 colours
available per 8x8 block of pixels. C64s had similarly poor graphics
power, although they allowed a bit more control over colour at the
expense of resolution. Code size was also kept small because these CPUs
were 8-bit, with instructions often being only a single byte in length.

The 16-bit era saw some experimentation with data cacheing, but since
nobody wanted to upgrade their machines *at all*, it left our options
limited. (Although I recall Sales Curve experimenting with on-the-fly
level loading from floppy disks in "SWIV".) But this was a limited
option at best, since floppies didn't hold much data. 720Kb per disk was
not all that much space when you had to load data that was much bigger
than we had in the 8-bit days. There's a major leap from 2-colour XOR
sprites to 16-colour cookie-cut masked sprite data. That increase alone
was enough to make 512Kb actually feel *smaller* than the 48K available
on a Sinclair Spectrum.

Christ, it took *years* before gamers could be persuaded to upgrade from
512K to 1Mb -- a process that cost upwards of 100 UK pounds back then.
(Today, for comparison, 512Kb RAM would cost less than a pound.)

It is feasible to use a sort of 'fake virtual memory' using floppies --
my last project *had* to do this, since the data, even when compressed,
simply wouldn't fit in the computer's 2Mb RAM. (It was written for the
Amiga A1200 series.) Unfortunately, floppies are hardly 'fast' and,
because the Amiga A1200 *still* had only a 900(ish)Kb/floppy limit, we
were forced to use twin drives to supply the full amount of virtual
memory required. To say this was slow is an understatement, but the
target market simply refused to buy hard disks.


On PCs, this problem is no longer applicable since access to hard drives
(and even CD-ROMs) is far faster than access to serialised files on
tape. Even the PC's standard floppies are twice the capacity of those of
the Amiga. In addition, we can cache at multiple levels: CD-ROM -> Hard
drive -> Memory (->CPU cache). This gives us far more flexibility in our
data handling. And since, contrary to 'Net users' expectations, CD-ROM
is the standard delivery medium for games, this gives us *shitloads* of
space for data storage, and plenty of flexibility as to how we access
it.

[I won't deny that there are 'lazy' installation programs that insist on
transferring the entire 200+Mb of data from CD to hard disk, but this
*is* necessary if the music is supplied via traditional Red Book audio
CD tracks. You can't play Red Book audio tracks *and* read data from a
CD at the same time because of hardware limitations.]

Today, the code:data ratio is actually decreasing as graphics cards and
sound cards have improved dramatically since those early Trident VGA and
Ad-lib days. (FM synthesised music is tiny when compared to samples.) A
VGA display only needed 64Kb RAM for a back-buffer and the data for the
graphics was a lot less back then. 256 colours [obviously] requires only
1 byte per pixel. 24-bit textures require three times that space *and*
the resolutions we're using today are higher.

Even with a tripling in code size, it's still only a tiny percentage of
the total footprint of a modern game.

The use of bytes (or even 32-bit ints) for flagging may seem like
overkill, but these days, the support structures for a game are easily
dwarfed by the sheer quantity of graphics and sound data. For instance,
a simple square wall, defined in 3D space, would consist of 99% texture
map and 1% (if that) of actual descriptive data. Four sets of
coordinates (assuming a quadrilateral), node links (assuming a variety
of linked list structure), flags and a link to the mipmapping data will
take up only a few bytes.

Even if it took up 100 bytes, this is still *nothing* in comparison to
the graphics data itself. Mipmapping requires multiple textures *per
object*, so compressing the support structures isn't going to save you
much more than, say, 100Kb of space. Not a lot in the grand scheme of
things, and hardly worth spending days trying to compress.

One advantage to using a full WORD or DWORD space to hold relatively
small values is speed. Today's CPUs penalise the programmer if data sits
at an odd-numbered address, eating up extra cycles because it has to
read two batches of data from the RAM chips instead of one. Since speed
is of the essence, it often makes more sense to align data structures so
that important, frequently accessed data sits on DWORD boundaries. The
extra space this requires is insignificant considering the size of the
graphics.

Even with tile-mapped wargames, this speed-over-size thinking can pay
off. If you have a lot of AI working in the background, speeding up its
access to the data allows you to do a whole hell of a lot more. Add more
units; more effects; more datatypes... more gameplay. (The display code
in these games is relatively trivial in comparison to the AI.)

I hope this has answered a few questions.

[...]

--
Sean Timarco Baggaley

<mailto:stim...@cix.co.uk>

E&OE

> and edx,000000ffh ;5v EDX = green
> add ecx,ecx ;6u **red 1**
> nop ;6v
> lea edi,[edx*2+edx] ;7u **green 1**

; This is an AGI too
; EDX is used 3 lines ago. At least according to my docs, it will stall the
pipeline too

[snip rest of code]
>
>If I've analyzed the pairing correctly above, the code fragment posted
>should do the exact same thing in 18 cycles, not 26. There's only one AGI
>stall in it (which I didn't even discover until just now... DOH!), whereas
>the VC++5 code fragment has 9.
It's 19 cycles, according to my count. But it does not really matter. Even
for 18 cycles, you've got a speedup of 50% for the ASM compared to the C.
You claimed 200%, which is quite a difference. I just wanted to point out
that todays C compilers are getting closer and closer.

Apart from that, on a P233, this fragment would have to load data with 50
MB/s, not talking about the bandwidth needed for storing.
So your code is going to be bandwidth limited on high end machines.

Yes, I do understand that many people don't have a P233. But that's a matter
of time. In two years, your assembly code will not differ speed-wise from my
C++ code due to memory bandwidth problems. And it will probably violate
optimization rules for the newest high end systems then.

This is not really an issue with your single code fragment, since it is
quickly rewritten. In fact, it proves that well dosed usage of assembly can
be a benefit. But it also proves that writing the majority of code in
assembly is pure nonsense.

>Compilers have come a long way, and for this reason, writing large chunks
>of code in asm no longer makes sense; but when it comes down to hard coding
>the tight routines, the HumanBrain(tm) compiler always wins.
At least for now :-)

But yes, you're right there. Thus anybody in his right mind on this thread
stated you should code it in a high level language, profile, and optimize
the hot spots.

Bye,
Robert

Brian Shields
unread,
Mar 6, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

> >You need SOME of the game to be heavily optimized. Not all. Most code
> >is non-critical. Would you really make your initialization code in
> >asm instead of C?
>
> I have agreed that non crucial code can be written in C, though as
> you may have guessed, I *would* write that init routine in asm. I am
> not sure what it is, but I tend to associate bad coding with C. The
> number of terrible init routines I have seen, only goes to re-enforce
> this view. There seems to be a great deal to be said for spending
> *more* time on a bit of code. Asm forces you to do just that, and as
> such means you end up with a much more robust bit of work. C lets
> you get away with murder, and many people do just that.
Bad coding is bad coding, full stop. You can't blame a language for
that. If there is one thing I'm worried about with the decline in use of
asm it's that people don't understand what's going on inside the
machine. That's an argument for learning the internals, but NOT for
making 100% use of asm.
Besides, doesn't asm 'encourage' some really bad practices? Program
structure is a joke in asm. It's a mess of branches. Well optimised code
(esp. paired instructions for a Pentium or other multi-ALU CPU) is
virtually unreadable. You certainly can't look at a piece of code and
say "hey, that's a bubble sort routine" as easily as you can in C.
And try working between people with asm. Nightmare. And if one of your
team quits, it'll take so long to sort through their routines you may as
well re-write them.
> >First, asm is no longer necessary for many of the low-level tasks under
> >Windows.
>
> As long as you accept the overhead which Windows imposes.
DirectX routines are called as methods in a COM object. This takes 1
cyle more (I believe) than an subroutine branch in asm. And, of course,
this hands the operation over to dedicated hardware most of the time,
which will do the operation much faster, and the CPU can get on with
other things in the meantime. This is the fastest way you will ever be
able to get at hardware without hardcodeing hundreds of versions for
every combination of chipsets.

> >Second, when 1% of the code takes 99% of the runtime, why write the
> >other 99% in asm to gain perhaps a 0.5% speedup? Why not just write the
> >1% in asm to gain perhaps a 20-50% speedup?
>
> I agree, I *know* the actual programming in C is faster. I am
> arguing the other way since I see the code produced by C compilers as
> bloated and slow.
Take a look at some more modern compilers for a start. The code isn't
perfect (it could never be as the compiler has no realisation of what's
going on, what the most common situations are etc.) but they're pretty
good. Most optimisation these days is in the algorithm rather than the
indidvidual instructions. You explain it well to the C compiler and
it'll give you good code.

> >"yesteryear" is the key word. Windows totally shifted the game
> >programming model. You no longer do most of the low-level coding
> >yourself these days.
>
> You do if you think you can do it better. (faster/smaller/less
> buggy). I am fed up with having to change my computer every year, and
> going the Windows route forces this upon us, and our customers. In
> the 'good old days' :) computers didn't change every year, and yet,
> year by year the programs *did* get better... because the coders got
> better. Nowadays I fear that the coders will always rely on the next
> generation of computer to solve all their problems for them, rather
> than get a good book on Asm from the local library.

> >1. DirectX/OpenGL
>
> Is not really the issue here, it may be a big thing in the world of
> windows, but assembly programmers have had full video control for
> years.
Like I said, hardware outperforms the CPU many times over on a decent
CPU. Don't believe me? Try comparing OpenGL in software and hardware.
Try writing asoftware renderer that can do rgb channel colour and filter
textures in 640x480x16bpp. Keep a stopwatch ready so you can time how
long the frame takes to render ;-)

> >2. Sometimes C is "good enough"
> I agree already! I know C is good enough for certain routines. I
> am disagreeing with you as to *which* ones, and trying to show you how
> asm can help produce better code, even when speed is not an issue, as
> is the case with non-inner loop code.
>
> >5. C code is more readable, less buggy on average
>
> Hmm, more bugs seem to slip by the net with C, with asm, they tend
> to crash the computer, so the developer, rather than the end user, has
> to spend the time sorting them all out.
>
> >3. C compilers beat structured asm
> >4. Optimized (non-structured) asm takes too long to write
>
> "Takes too long to write". That is the answer to my question. Thank
> you.
"Takes to long to write" is a very practical question. What's the point
of having a project if by the time it's released it looks dated?

- Brian

to
Paul Hsieh wrote:
>; 13 clocks per iteration on a P54/P55. (Similar pairing, but 12 clocks on
>; an AMD K6 since branch target takes 0 clocks.)

I couldn't resist that one. The afternoon was going slow, and so I decided
to continue optimizing.
The following should go down the pipeline in 10 clocks on a P55. As an
additional benefit, it has no branches :-)

(At least according to my docs)

; Notation:
; Reg=1.2.3.4 denotes register split in bytes
; Reg=[1][2] denotes register split in words
mov eax,[ebp+8] ; EAX=?.R.G.B
xor ebx,ebx ; EBX=0
mov edx,eax ; EDX=?.R.G.B
and eax,00ff00ff ; EAX=[R][B]
mov bl,dh ; EBX=[][G]
mov esi,eax ; ESI=[R][B]
xor edi,edi ; EDI=0
lea edx,[eax+8*eax] ; EDX=[R*9][B*9]
lea ecx,[ebx+2*ecx] ; ECX=[G*3]
lea ebx,[ebx+8*ebx] ; EBX=[G*9]
shl ecx,6 ; ECX=[G*192]
lea eax,[edx+2*edx] ; EAX=[R*27][G*27]
lea esi,[esi+2*edx] ; ESI=[R*19][G*19]
sub ecx,ebx ; ECX=[183*G]
shr eax,15 ; EAX=[54*R]
add ecx,esi ; ECX=[183*G+19*B]
add eax,ecx ; EAX=54*R+183*G+19*B
shr eax,8 ; EAX=Y

Could any of the optimization Gurus check if I'm right there?

If this thread proves one thing, it proves that r.g.p. can come in handy
some times. Avery got a 40% speedup for free :-)

Bye,
Robert

>It's not an AGI stall. AGI stalls occur whenever the effective address of
>an instruction is modified in the previous cycle. That's why they say 3
>instructions back; if you space the instructions more than 3 apart, they

Jason Shankel's profile photo
Jason Shankel
unread,
Mar 6, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Sean Timarco Baggaley wrote:

> I have to partially disagree with this statement. Why should 3D
> acceleration kill alternative approaches? You'll still need 2D support,
> if only to run the OS the game uses.
>
> If you want to play with Voxel Space tech, go ahead and do it. Nothing's
> stopping you.
If you want to interleave 2d elements (sprites, GUI controls, whatever),
on many hardware platforms you have two choices:
a. decompose your 2d stuff into polygons, which means blowing lots of
texture space on non-3d stuff and forcing your 2d elements to be 2^n*2^m
where m<8 && n<8 or
b. take an lfb lock and stall the hell out of your 3d pipeline.

Lots of the new 3d hardware isn't supporting palettized textures,
eliminating palette scrolling effects for texture animations, a
tried-and-true 2d technique

Texture memory is incredibly limited compared to the gobs of system
memory. For highly complicated scenes, which might not be a problem in
software, you can find yourself having to upload multiple megs of
textures per frame to the card, which is not going to get you any high
framerate awards.

Procedural textures, and other customized per-pixel texture filters are
impossible when you don't have a pointer to the texture surface at
rasterization time.

Basically, since 3d hardware takes rasterization out of software and
because hardware surface manipulation does not peacefully coexist with
software surface manipulation, your choices become limited.

That 3d rasterizer does bucketloads more than you could hope for in
software, so the tradeoff is usually worth it. But it is a tradeoff.

Sean T Barrett's profile photo
Sean T Barrett
unread,
Mar 6, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

>Sean Timarco Baggaley wrote:
>> Why should 3D acceleration kill alternative approaches?
>> If you want to play with Voxel Space tech, go ahead and do it. Nothing's
>> stopping you.
It doesn't kill it, it simply makes it unattractive.
Suppose I write three engines in software, and the
way it works out they all look kinda neat in different
ways and are basically cool and similar frame rates
at the same resolution:
texture-mapped polygon engine
voxelspace engine
real-time raytracing engine (just suppose, for the moment)

Now... with hardware acceleration, that texture
mapping polygon engine runs at 2x the speed, in
a higher resolution, and with bilinear filtering
which makes 95% of the pixels onscreen anti-aliased.
Suddenly one of those engines looks a hell of a lot
better than the others.

Not that anybody is going to do it that way, it's
more a matter of across-game competition. Most
technological graphics advances have come from
technology-driven games--games whose "wow" factor
have come from the technology. There's not much
motivation to research other different technology
if there's not going to be any "wow" factor because
99% of the people will think a lores engine (or
whatever the drawback is) looks like crap.

There may well be other techniques than texture
mapped polygons which for certain scene complexities
would give better performance for the same appearance
(e.g. voxels for super-bumpy objects)--if they were
running on the same hardware. But the software
renderers don't have hand-tailored hardware pixel
pipelines providing exactly the necessary functional
units to maintain high-throughput, with deep read-ahead
pipelines to avoid the latency from memory, with parallel
control structures so that there isn't an enormous
pipeline flush at the end of every span of rendered
pixels (or whatever the inner loops is). Software
rasterization of texture-mapped polygons can't compete
with hardware rasterization--not for with same quality
(bilerping, per-pixel mipmapping) and feature-set (alpha,
etc); nobody has any reason to believe that other software
rendering technologies can suddenly outrun software
polygon rendering by the 4x or so that's probably
necessary to compete with hardware; if it could have,
it would have come up before. (And, no, Commanche's
voxelspace couldn't compete. They rendered 160x100
for a game that was in 320x200.)

Sean Barrett

In article <EpF8u...@world.std.com>, buz...@world.std.com says...
Your point is correct, that some approaches are much less attractive now,
but...

There is *some* variety in 3D hardware. (Unfortunately to date it's
mostly been different varieties of crap. :-) This could provide an
opportunity for an offbeat "wow" factor technique to promote more
flexible or unusual hardware.

One thing I'd like to see in 3D hardware is fast, graceful access from
the CPU to texture/rendering surface memory, to do some of the hybrid
tricks that Jason Shankel mentioned. If one reasonably popular chip
supplies this in a way to better support a "wow" factor game, it could
create pressure on other vendors to improve that aspect of their designs.
Look at what glQuake has done for alpha-blending.

The ball's in our court.

Speaking as one of those names, we sure do. Something happened the other
day at work which pleased me greatly: my co-workers recognized me for
(consistantly) writing some of the best object oriented code in our
game(s). Sure, I wrote 13K lines of ASM for AoE, but I also contributed

development efforts.

designs are all part of what it takes to not just start a game, but
finish it, and make it something special. We can all pick a absolute
and run this thread to infinity, but why?

Geoff Cramond and David Braben started in the days where it was a black
and white choice, 8 bit Basic or Assembler if you liked the idea of a
100x speedup and 10% more RAM. I don't know either of these guys but I
do know other programmers who started on 8 bit machines who even now
only know two languages: Basic and Assembler, I was lucky in that I
started just late enough (87) on the Amiga to be using C for tools and
stuff (still used 100% assembler for the actual games though). It's also
worth pointing out that Frontier was Amiga first, weren't team 17 100%
ASM coders when everything was Amiga first?, I know we were, Populous 2
was our last C free project.

Glenn Corpes's profile photo
Glenn Corpes

to

stbag...@hotmail.com (Sean Timarco Baggaley) wrote:
> In article <memo.19980305...@frog.compulink.co.uk>, Glenn
> Corpes <gle...@cix.co.uk.notthisbityoumoron> writes
>
> [...]
>
> >That's bullshit, sure hardware acceleration sadly stops a few
> >interesting graphic approaches but it opens up a lot of others.
>

> I have to partially disagree with this statement. Why should 3D
> acceleration kill alternative approaches? You'll still need 2D support
> ,
> if only to run the OS the game uses.
>

> If you want to play with Voxel Space tech, go ahead and do it. Nothing
> 's
> stopping you.

We had a couple of prototype technologies stomped out by 3D
acceleration, one was a huge cache destroying block of code (260k) with
all the movs for every possible 'small' triangle hardcoded in it, with a
different colour for each vertex. This could draw 8000 polygons at 60
FPS on a pentium 90, you need a lot of hardware to do that with an
accelerator. We also had a textured, curved surface draw which was many
times faster than breaking the surface up into small triangles. That
said, it was pretty clear that these techniques wouldn't stand up to
comparison with high res, filtered, 16 bit accelerated triangles. As I
said, 3D hardware does open up other opportunities, texture generation
and LOD systems are our current areas of research.

Glenn Corpes wrote:
> Geoff Cramond and David Braben started in the days where it was a
> black and white choice, 8 bit Basic or Assembler if you liked the
> idea of a 100x speedup and 10% more RAM. I don't know either of
> these guys but I do know other programmers who started on 8 bit

> machines who even now only know two languages: Basic and Assembler.

True enough. I started out programming (albeit not professionally)
when there was no other alternative either, but I constantly look for
better programming tools that can be used. I think a lot of hardcore
'old-timers' are stuck in their old ways, and just won't accept that
high-level languages are acceptable. I certainly respect Braben/Crammond
for what they've done. They've been far more successful than *I* could
probably ever hope to be, but I don't really agree with their (previous,
anyway) development strategy.

I mean, who in their right mind would write a game like Comanche 3 in
assembly? Christ, there must be so much logic in there that'll be
unreadable unless they use tons of macros/higher-level constructs in
MASM. And if they did use macros/MASM features then they might as well
have used C or C++.

I do realise at the end of the day, to the end-user it doesn't really
matter what language a game is written in or how unelegantly the program
is designed, as long as it's a good game. However, using a more
appropriate tool would make the life more easier for the developer. But
you already know this, obviously.

I mean, why isn't there a 3Dfx patch for Comanche3, F1GP2 or Frontier
yet? (or is there?)

> I was lucky in that I started just late enough (87) on the Amiga to
> be using C for tools and stuff (still used 100% assembler for the
> actual games though).

Hmm... Yeah I guess the same applies to me, although I didn't really
program professionally back then. Most of the sample code for the Amiga
was in C so I more or less had to learn to decipher the language before
I could do anything interesting (couldn't afford a C book). It was quite
an interesting and exciting challenge, actually ;-). It wasn't really
until SAS/C V6 came out that I totally adopted C on the Amiga.

> It's also worth pointing out that Frontier was Amiga first, weren't
> team 17 100% ASM coders when everything was Amiga first?, I know we
> were, Populous 2 was our last C free project.

Oh yes. We were hardcore ASM men back then, although I used C for
some components towards the end. Nowadays, the games that actually get
released from here seem to be written mainly in C++.

In fact, I think Andreas used one of the ugliest hacks *ever*
(actually, the whole game was an ugly quick'n dirty hack :-) to get
Amiga "Project X" to master in time: He had a problem where the game
would crash randomly on starting a game; So he inserted some code to
flash the LED (and nothing else), to determine where the code crashed,
and found that it DIDN'T crash anymore. So he left it in, and that's why
the game flashes the LED for a second or two at the start of a level. It
must've been some race condition with an interrupt or something, but he
never bothered to find out. I could't believe that they shipped like
that, but there you go... No-one seemed to notice ;) "Good old days",
eh?

I've seen some strange things here:
1. Some of you seem to think asm is hard to learn. It isn't. You only
need to know a few instructions to start programming, and you need a
few references for the OS and (S)VGA. To learn C(++), you need to
memorize lots of functions. It's easy to write asm, but it's difficult
to optimize it for every different processor.

2. Some things are easier in asm: when you need low-level access to
the hardware, a high level language tends to remove you from the
machine, into it's own world of abstractions.

3. Some people say: when you have some unused processor time, use it
to add extra features.
I don't agree. When you have finished a project, and then start adding
things, you're probably adding bugs too. It's hard to add things
unless you have designed the program's structure to accept the
changes. Otherwise, a little change in A might require a little change
in B which might require... You'll end up rewriting the whole thing.
Besides, I think every large programming project should start on a
sheet of paper (or lots of sheets, maybe). Having an idea and
immediately jumping towards the compiler might be a bad idea.

4. When your program is too slow, use a profiler and optimise where it
matters. And know your profiler.

5. Optimising, or even completely redesigning an algorithm is probably
faster than simply translating something to asm.

6. Ever tried Windows programming in asm? Not impossible, but...

I wonder what Pentium owners think when they see:
required: 486.
a) Well, if it runs fast on this thing, it will be even faster on a
Pentium.
b) What??? 486??? I don't buy this, It's outdated.

The majority of customers are like lemmings jumping off cliffs (Win95,
a new computer every year,... for example). If we want to sell
something, we might as well think about writing programs for them.

Here we go again...
Wim Libaers wrote in message <6e9fo0$o0j$3...@news1.skynet.be>...


>I've seen some strange things here:
>
>1. Some of you seem to think asm is hard to learn. It isn't.

It is.

>You only need to know a few instructions to start
>programming, and you need a few references for the OS
>and (S)VGA.

If you only know a couple of instructions then you most
definitely should NOT be using asm. What is the point
of using asm if you aren't capable of out-optimising a
compiler? That's the point that most asm advoxates
seem to miss - asm is only as good as the coder.

>To learn C(++), you need to memorize lots of functions.

Not really. There are plenty of good API references. In
terms of pure memorisation, the number of instructions
and the scope of the knowledge required (cycle counts,
pairing, stalls) asm is a far greater challenge than C's
tiny set of operators.

>It's easy to write asm, but it's difficult to optimize it for every
>different processor.

That's kinda the point, though, isn't it? What use is non-optimal
asm? If I wanted to make life difficult for the sake of it, I'd use
INTERCAL.

>2. Some things are easier in asm:

Like what?

>when you need low-level access to the hardware, a high level
>language tends to remove you from the machine, into it's own
>world of abstractions.

Really? C is a systems programming language. It has all the
power of asm for pointer/memory handling. DOS C compilers
have instructions to call interrupts and write to hardware ports.
There is *nothing* I've seen done in asm that can't be done in
C - the only difference is how much overhead gets added
(which, IME, is not particularly significant due to the speed of
the INT and IN/OUT instructions).

>3. Some people say: when you have some unused processor
>time, use it to add extra features.
>I don't agree. When you have finished a project, and then start
>adding things, you're probably adding bugs too. It's hard to
>add things unless you have designed the program's structure
>to accept the changes.

This is an argument for asm?

>Otherwise, a little change in A might require a little change in
>B which might require... You'll end up rewriting the whole thing.
>Besides, I think every large programming project should start
>on a sheet of paper (or lots of sheets, maybe). Having an
>idea and immediately jumping towards the compiler might
>be a bad idea.

Maybe, but I'd sooner jump in and code a new idea and see
how well it goes than trying to reason everything out first.
If it works, great, if not just restore the backups.

This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the ability
to try out new approaches and algorithms without getting
bogged down in optimising the code and making it fit
optimally with the rest of the code.

>4. When your program is too slow, use a profiler and optimise
>where it matters. And know your profiler.
>
>5. Optimising, or even completely redesigning an algorithm
>is probably faster than simply translating something to asm.

Yup. Both of these are valuable points to note.

>6. Ever tried Windows programming in asm? Not impossible,
>but...

Worthless? Yup. Simulating the different calling conventions
in asm isn't going to make the functions return any faster.

---
Russ

Brandon Van Every wrote in message
