>>
>> It is.
>
>Oh come off it. C++ (in its entireity) has *way* more of a
>learning curve than ASM.
But does anyone *need* to know C++ in it's entirety?
(For that matter *does* anyone know C++ in it's entirety?)

>> If you only know a couple of instructions then you most
>> definitely should NOT be using asm. What is the point
>> of using asm if you aren't capable of out-optimising a
>> compiler?
>
>Heard of RISC?

Yes. I guess there's a fine point with RISC, but this thread
is about x86. How could RISC asm be the language of
choice when the dominant platform is CRISC?

Even so, RISC CPUs still have optimising rules and it's
still possible to generate worse code through naivete. The
instructions still need pairing and the 'instruction after a
jump is always executed' can be a right gotcha.

RISC code is certainly easier to get right, but it's not a
given and compilers on RISC tend to get a lot closer
to optimal - more registers and simple ops mean there's
less room to improve. No carry-flag tricks, for example.

[...]


>> >To learn C(++), you need to memorize lots of functions.
>>
>> Not really. There are plenty of good API references. In
>> terms of pure memorisation, the number of instructions
>> and the scope of the knowledge required (cycle counts,
>> pairing, stalls) asm is a far greater challenge than C's
>> tiny set of operators.
>
>Agreed. But are we discussing learning C vs. learning
>ASM, or learning how to do *fast* code in either language?

Either. Both. I'd say that learning to write sub-compiler
quality asm is probably not much use (except as a step).

>You still have to know about cycle counts to make the C
>code go fast.

Not specifically. You just need to know things like:
* indirecting through a pointer is slower than direct
addressing, but faster than copying the data.
* function pointers are slower than function calls
* multiply/divide slow, shifts/add/subtract/logic fast
* ifs/cases bad, jump tables good
Those rules apply to probably every CPU in existence
to varying degrees. It doesn't tell you the specifics of
the CPU, but fast C tends to stay fast when ported.

Pure dumb luck is an option, too. A good compiler and an
idiosyncratic bit of coding can get near-asm speeds without
the coder having the faintest idea why. Naturally, I wouldn't
recommend this...


[...]


>> >when you need low-level access to the hardware, a high
>> >level language tends to remove you from the machine,
>> >into it's own world of abstractions.
>>
>> Really? C is a systems programming language. It has all
>> the power of asm for pointer/memory handling. DOS C
>> compilers have instructions to call interrupts and write to
>> hardware ports. There is *nothing* I've seen done in asm
>> that can't be done in C - the only difference is how much
>> overhead gets added (which, IME, is not particularly
>> significant due to the speed of the INT and IN/OUT
>> instructions).

>Yeah, I agree that we don't need ASM to write device drivers.

But you still use it anyway (or at least the Win95 DDK samples
do).

Then again, I'd guess that an average driver writer is a damn
sight more competent in asm than an average DOS coder.

>Who sez we gotta bring every CPU to its knees?

That's just a bonus... ;)

>What's wrong with allowing something to run on a slightly
>older, slower computer because full power wasn't required?

Well, the people with the faster machines (who buy the most
games) want their cool graphical tidbits...
If a game is finished and works on lesser hardware then,
so what? It's not like you want to throw away market share.
If the game does what it was designed to, with all the little
things to make power-users go "Cool!", then it working on
a P133 is hardly a problem (the publisher will put 'required:
P166' on the box, anyway...)

>> This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the ability

On 13 Mar 1998 11:46:27 GMT, "Brandon Van Every" <vane...@blarg.net> wrote:

And what exactly is this supposed to mean? That RISC CPUs
somehow would be harder to program in assembler is a myth
perpetrated by people who have never programmed RISC CPUs
in assembler.

MIPS R3000A as used in Sony Playstation is _trivial_ to
program in assembler. Compare this to a CISC Pentium II
which is a pain in the butt, and your statement is reduced
to nothing but a sweeping generalization that's utterly
utterly wrong.

On 13 Mar 1998 11:46:27 GMT, vane...@blarg.net says...

> Russ Williams wrote:
> > Here we go again...
> > Wim Libaers wrote:
> > >I've seen some strange things here:
> > >
> > >1. Some of you seem to think asm is hard to learn. It isn't.
> >
> > It is.
>
> Oh come off it. C++ (in its entireity) has *way* more of a learning curve
> than ASM.
It all depends on how you would assess how much you have "learned" about
a particular language. For example, I don't consider my C knowledge is
even complete *without* my knowledge of assembly language.

If on the other hand you are just concerned about getting things done,
they again you have to look at the application. The scope of assembly is
larger than that of C, so while C can take you to some places a lot
faster, it has a hard time taking you to some places at all.

> > If you only know a couple of instructions then you most
> > definitely should NOT be using asm. What is the point
> > of using asm if you aren't capable of out-optimising a
> > compiler?
>
> Heard of RISC?

:o) I used to think this too. An Arm programmer and PPC programmer have
since set me straight. RISC assembly does not necessarily mean "few"
instructions, or even "easy to understand" instructions. (Those ARM
people, in particular, has some really peculiar ideas.)

> > That's the point that most asm advoxates
> > seem to miss - asm is only as good as the coder.
>
> We're agreed here.

But all you are really saying is that if your programming skills are
worse than those who wrote your C compiler and worse than the limitations
of the compiler implementation due to pragmatic constraints, then your C
compiler won't be the source of your performance bottlenecks.

For those people, sure stick to the C compiler. But to someone who is
serious about performance, this is not the central issue.

> > >To learn C(++), you need to memorize lots of functions.
> >
> > Not really. There are plenty of good API references. In
> > terms of pure memorisation, the number of instructions
> > and the scope of the knowledge required (cycle counts,
> > pairing, stalls) asm is a far greater challenge than C's
> > tiny set of operators.

"Pairing" only applies to the now obsolete Pentium Processor. Optimizing
for the P6/K6 family of processors is different, and in general easier to
get good results with (though admittedly, significantly harder to
understand all the *why* questions.)

> Agreed. But are we discussing learning C vs. learning ASM, or learning how

> to do *fast* code in either language? You still have to know about cycle


> counts to make the C code go fast.

Well, the problem is, you have to know a hell of a lot more than just
cycle counts to make C go fast. That is assuming that you want to
actually get beyond the peformance of your C compiler. If you are given
C loop, can you guess what stream of assembly your compiler will given
you? While you can disassemble your one piece of code to see, how will
you know if another C loop is faster or slower without coding it up,
compiling and disassembling it, and comparing? Seems a lot harder than
just starting with the answer in direct assembly language.

If you want to reach this level of performance you have to actually know
assembly anyways.

> > >2. Some things are easier in asm:
> >
> > Like what?
>
> Very fast math loops that must be tightly scheduled to get the last 20% of
> performance.

I remember an article written somewhere (I can't remember the source)
which compared implementing a huffman encoder in C versus assembly. It
turned out that the assembly version was shorter, easier to understand
and a hell of a lot faster.

While C is usually the easier route, you should take such generalizations
such "C is always easier than assembly" with a grain of salt.

Here's a question for you kiddies, how in C would you implement a
lightning fast "mod 96"? That is, optimize the following function:

int MOD96(unsigned int x) {
return x % 96;
}

Let's say you are also given that you know x is between 0 and 2^24, from
context. You're a little hard pressed given that its really only 1 C
statement aren't you? To see the answer in assembly see:

http://www.pobox.com/~qed/adiv.html

You aren't exactly going to duplicate that in an C encoding.

> > >when you need low-level access to the hardware, a high level
> > >language tends to remove you from the machine, into it's own
> > >world of abstractions.
> >
> > Really? C is a systems programming language. It has all the
> > power of asm for pointer/memory handling. DOS C compilers
> > have instructions to call interrupts and write to hardware ports.
> > There is *nothing* I've seen done in asm that can't be done in
> > C - the only difference is how much overhead gets added
> > (which, IME, is not particularly significant due to the speed of
> > the INT and IN/OUT instructions).
>
> Yeah, I agree that we don't need ASM to write device drivers.

Well I don't! How do you write a MUTEX in C? You know that thing where
you do an atom compare and exchange? (Look up cmpxchg, if you don't know
what I am talking about.)

What if you want to trap 16 bit and 32 bit interrupts and reroute them
both to a particular bitness (i.e., a bimodal trap)? Admittedly this
could be done with mostly C, and using the standard extensions in the
Pmode APIs, but now lets say you wanted to trap *ALL 256* such
interrupts. You either start running out of memory real fast, or you
write some clever assembly to solve the problem.

> > This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the ability
> > to try out new approaches and algorithms without getting
> > bogged down in optimising the code and making it fit
> > optimally with the rest of the code.

C's only advantage here is that it may be a little faster, since you have
all of C's built in assumptions that you can fall back on.

The real advantage of C, in my opinion, is that it comes with standard
libraries and uses a standardized calling conventions that are abstracted
from the programming, which is for most of your coding is usually what
you want. (It allows you to save the petal to the metal optimization
effort for the places your profiler says you need it.)

> Yes, agreed! Always prototype in C before committing to ASM. If nothing
> else, you will have a debugged ASM dump for your final ASM code.

Well, we all have our methods for coding. Personally when I am tinkering
around with little programs, I find myself look at Ralph Brown's
interrupt list as much as I do C lib help files.

--
Paul Hsieh
q...@pobox.com

algorithmes, you would NEVER use in C! Small Code. Faster assembling then

SWI "OS_WriteS": "Hello World"+CHR$(0): SWI "OS_Exit"

>Use a RISC-processor like the ARM-family (3DO, Newton,
>Acorn Archimedes/RiscPC) Small, easy to learn, efficient
>and powerfull instruction-/registerset.

And almost no room for improvement over even a competent
compiler. Scheduling ops is a remarkably easy task with
lots of registers and a few, simple opcodes. In fact, I'd say
making the best use of 16/24/32/64/128 general registers is
far easier for a compiler than a human.

Consider the 'U2 crossing a bridge' problem that has been
floating around. Most people simply give up and start making
jokes about U2. There are 72 solutions for the minimum
number of moves, which would take microseconds for a
program to search exhaustively. Computers are good at
scheduling problems.

>> What is the point of using asm if you aren't capable of
>> out-optimising a compiler?
>
>Fun, for example.

I suppose. I certainly wouldn't enjoy losing to a compiler, though.

>Get experience in ASM-programming, to get able to
>out-optimising a compiler.

Perhaps. I'm not sure how that would be more useful than
reading and understanding compiler output, though.

>Use processor-dependent data-structures and algorithmes,
>you would NEVER use in C!

Example?

>Small Code.

Most compilers can optimise for size. The RTL and
bindings add quite some overhead, but if you view the
generated asm, it's usually pretty compact. No-one says
you can't 'cheat' and let the compiler do most of the work
then assemble it yourself...

>Faster assembling then compiling,

Hasn't been a significant problem in 3 decades.

>BBC-BasicV on the RiscPC has an Inline-assembler,
>but no c-compiler ;-) etc...

OK. That's one good reason for asm (and BASIC, too).

>For example, on the Acorn RiscPC under RiscOS, a
>Hello-World binaery is 2 Instructions and 20 bytes long...
>
>SWI "OS_WriteS": "Hello World"+CHR$(0): SWI "OS_Exit"
>
>I've not found ONE compiler for RiscOS, that could do that.

Well, I don't know about those compilers, but most PC
compilers I've seen would generate something like:

String1: db 'Hello World',0
;_main
push offset String1
call _printf
ret 0

replacing the null in the string with a $, the call _printf with
an int 21/function 9 call and assembling in tiny mode to a
.COM file would manage a 20 byte program:
mov ah,9
mov dx,108
int 21
ret
db 'Hello World$'

It does involve some hand asm, but how else would you
change the RTL call to an OS call? For non-trivial code,
almost all of the program can be generated by the
compiler, with only a little modification needed to make
it assemble and run.

>>>It's easy to write asm, but it's difficult to optimize it for every
>>>different processor.
>
>> That's kinda the point, though, isn't it? What use is non-optimal
>> asm?
>
>To enable a specification, that contains a minimum plattform,
>to run your programme at full speed?
>
>Say, if I'd like to write a game, that have to run on a 133 Mhz
>Pentium, with 2 MB SVGA-Card at full speed, I don't have to
>code optimal pentium pro asm, 'cause there's no need to run
>it faster, then it's optimal speed.

Problem: Given that it would be run on a multitasking OS
(Win95 - in a DOS box if necessary), we don't want to waste
cycles for other tasks. That would be rude. So why not
code in C (taking less time and effort) and let the compiler
produce tight, near-optimal code? There is no excuse for
willfully generating poor code when a compiler is available
and could do better.

>> Maybe, but I'd sooner jump in and code a new idea and see
>> how well it goes than trying to reason everything out first.
>> If it works, great, if not just restore the backups.
>
>As long, as the module/objects and it's interfaces etc. between
>them are well defined before...

Why? I mean, sure, it's easier that way, but interfaces are no
more difficult to change than anything else. You just have to
be a little more careful when checking in the changes...

>> This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the ability
>> to try out new approaches and algorithms without getting
>> bogged down in optimising the code and making it fit
>> optimally with the rest of the code.
>
>If you code in ASM, you have both choices:
>Think about the algorithm, and/or it's best technical
>detail/datastructure/implementation.

Of course. That's because pipelined, stall-free asm is *so*
easy to generate? Removing ONE instruction from a
medium-sized routine of Pentium asm is likely to mean at
least half an hour's work re-pairing the ops and making
sure no stalls appear. A similar change in C would take
half a minute and would produce nearly the same code.

>Why, for example, should I use a bresenham
>linedraw-algorithm, when I know, there's a faster way in
>assembler, then a asm-bresenham-linedraw?

Why do you think that you can't code this other method in
C? I suppose in that case, it's probably a carry-flag
trick which is THE place to make compiler-beating
optimisations.

Even so, that's why we have inline asm. It most certainly
doesn't make "ASM the game language of choice".

to

Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article
<889899583.24334.0...@news.demon.co.uk>...

> Andreas Joos wrote in message ...
> >
> >Use a RISC-processor like the ARM-family (3DO, Newton,
> >Acorn Archimedes/RiscPC) Small, easy to learn, efficient
> >and powerfull instruction-/registerset.
>
> And almost no room for improvement over even a competent
> compiler. Scheduling ops is a remarkably easy task with
> lots of registers and a few, simple opcodes. In fact, I'd say
> making the best use of 16/24/32/64/128 general registers is
> far easier for a compiler than a human.
Well you can say it, but that doesn't make it true. :-) How much actual
hands-on experience do you have with these compilers, to back up your
claim?

I can't speak for ARM, but I know a lot about VC++ for DEC Alpha.
Scheduling is pretty good for mathematical stuff. But, it is not tight
enough to handle cases when you've run out of registers and you're clearing
up calculations on-the-fly in a tight loop to make available space. e.g. a
4x4 (matrix * vector) transform executing once every 16 cycles, where you
don't want to re-load the matrix every time. In general, register capacity
will always run out at some point, and the compiler will always choke at
some point. It is in the nature of human speed-demons to push a CPU
architecture to the limit, so this will always be a problem for any
compiler and CPU.

Also, there will be processing/scheduling tricks that seem easy enough to
you as a human, but that the compiler writer wasn't planning on.
Simultaneously processing (X) in both the FPU and the INT unit comes to
mind. In 3d graphics you frequently do boolean ops on the integer
representation of the FPU value to process stuff like trivial accept/reject
and other boundary conditions. On Alpha there are architectural penalties
for doing simultaneous float and int loads under certain conditions, and
the compiler doesn't synchronize the scheduling well enough to prevent
this. But hand-written ASM can do the job easily, you simply set up the
computational dependencies the way you actually want them to align
cycle-wise.

Finally, the ARM is a "predicated" architecture, most of the instructions
take an "if (X)" conditional as to whether they should be executed or not.
At this point in our computational history, I doubt that compiler writers
have spent a lot of time figuring out the potential scheduling benefits of
such instructions.

> >Get experience in ASM-programming, to get able to
> >out-optimising a compiler.
>
> Perhaps. I'm not sure how that would be more useful than
> reading and understanding compiler output, though.

The compiler output does not contain any performance data about why the
instructions are laid out as they are. And the quality of the layout is
only going to be as good as the compiler writer's ability to anticipate
your computational problem.

> >Say, if I'd like to write a game, that have to run on a 133 Mhz
> >Pentium, with 2 MB SVGA-Card at full speed, I don't have to
> >code optimal pentium pro asm, 'cause there's no need to run
> >it faster, then it's optimal speed.

True of games, for now. Not true of other markets like 3d animation/CAD,
nor will it be true of games next year.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

>But, it is not tight enough to handle cases when you've run

Ok, Brandon, what speedup would you expect if you were
to write a complete application (say, a game) in 100%
pure Alpha asm instead of using VC5/Alpha? And how
much of that could you get from just doing the hotspots?

>Finally, the ARM is a "predicated" architecture, most of the
>instructions take an "if (X)" conditional as to whether they
>should be executed or not.

ARM is predicated, I believe.

>At this point in our computational history, I doubt that
>compiler writers have spent a lot of time figuring out the
>potential scheduling benefits of such instructions.

They f**king well should have.
According to Intel's EPIC/IA64/Merced web pages, studies
have shown that predication is a major win (reducing branches
to 30%, IIRC).

I'd have thought that using predication for most 'if-else' statements
would be a remarkably obvious step to take in compiler
design.

>> >Get experience in ASM-programming, to get able to
>> >out-optimising a compiler.
>>
>> Perhaps. I'm not sure how that would be more useful than
>> reading and understanding compiler output, though.
>

>The compiler output does not contain any performance
>data about why the instructions are laid out as they are.

Neither does writing code. You need details of how the
CPU executes particular instructions and the optimisation
rules. These can (just about) be obtained by
experimentation, but looking at patterns in good code and
(more importantly) CPU docs is a better option.

I'd say it's certainly easier to learn how to write good x86
code by looking at compiler output, VTune, Intel's docs and
Agner Fog's optimisation docs than it is to figure it out by
writing naive code...

Paul Hsieh wrote in message ...

>It all depends on how you would assess how much you have
>"learned" about a particular language. For example, I don't
>consider my C knowledge is even complete *without* my

Probably not, but do you need complete C knowledge to
produce good code? I very much doubt it.

>If on the other hand you are just concerned about getting
>things done, they again you have to look at the application.
>The scope of assembly is larger than that of C, so while C
>can take you to some places a lot faster, it has a hard time
>taking you to some places at all.

True, but the difference in scope is vanishingly small. There
are very few things that require asm and (IMO) none that
require _pure_ asm.

[...]


>> > That's the point that most asm advoxates
>> > seem to miss - asm is only as good as the coder.
>>
>> We're agreed here.
>
>But all you are really saying is that if your programming
>skills are worse than those who wrote your C compiler
>and worse than the limitations of the compiler
>implementation due to pragmatic constraints, then your
>C compiler won't be the source of your performance
>bottlenecks.

But that's true for the vast majority of programmers...

[...]


>> > >To learn C(++), you need to memorize lots of functions.
>> >
>> > Not really. There are plenty of good API references. In
>> > terms of pure memorisation, the number of instructions
>> > and the scope of the knowledge required (cycle counts,
>> > pairing, stalls) asm is a far greater challenge than C's
>> > tiny set of operators.
>
>"Pairing" only applies to the now obsolete Pentium
>Processor.

No, it applies to any dual pipeline superscalar CPU.
The Pentium and R3000 are such CPUs. They're just
about obsolete, but still in use.

'Pairing' is, on the face of it, limited to dual-pipe CPUs,
but in the general case the same techniques are needed
on *all* superscalar CPUs. Quad pipeline RISC chips
require instructions arranging in groups of 4, for example.
It's the same as pairing, but with more pipes. 'Grouping',
perhaps?

>Optimizing for the P6/K6 family of processors is
>different, and in general easier to get good results
>with (though admittedly, significantly harder to
>understand all the *why* questions.)

The P6 is a pain because it rearranges everything, but it
would still (probably) show advantages if fed a stream of
instructions to keep as many execution units as possible
running in order.

The P6s are designed, as far as I can tell, to optimise the
code they're passed, making cache and decoding
optimisations the most beneficial.

>> Agreed. But are we discussing learning C vs. learning
>> ASM, or learning how to do *fast* code in either
>> language? You still have to know about cycle
>> counts to make the C code go fast.
>
>Well, the problem is, you have to know a hell of a lot more
>than just cycle counts to make C go fast. That is assuming
>that you want to actually get beyond the peformance of
>your C compiler.

That's the point, though, isn't it? The performance of the C
compiler is better than most people, so you need to know
plenty of asm to beat it significantly.

>If you are given C loop, can you guess what stream of
>assembly your compiler will given you?

Sometimes.

>While you can disassemble your one piece of code to
>see, how will you know if another C loop is faster or
>slower without coding it up, compiling and
>disassembling it, and comparing?

Does it matter? Using random statistical sampling (aka
occasionally looking at the disassembly), you can tell how
good the code compiler *generally* makes is. If the loops
are usually pretty optimal, then it will probably *not* be
shite in any one instance.

>Seems a lot harder than just starting with the answer in
>direct assembly language.

Sure, if you're anal enough to require that every 'for' loop
is optimal.

Inner loops? Yes, asm is good.

Outer loops? Would anyone miss a cycle or two on an
imperfect loop? No.

>If you want to reach this level of performance you have to
>actually know assembly anyways.

It helps. I don't think it's necessary. Then again, I know
asm, so it's difficult to say how much of that knowledge
I use subconciously when writing C...

>> > >2. Some things are easier in asm:
>> >
>> > Like what?
>>
>> Very fast math loops that must be tightly scheduled to get
>> the last 20% of performance.
>
>I remember an article written somewhere (I can't remember
>the source) which compared implementing a huffman
>encoder in C versus assembly.

Was it in DDJ? Or was that a decoder?

>It turned out that the assembly version was shorter, easier to
>understand and a hell of a lot faster.

Same algorithms? Or is it a case of redesign? That's the
sort of thing people usually do before proclaiming asm
to be much shorter, cleaner and faster.

A Huffman coder is probably an ideal application for
asm - you get the double-precision shifts and carry
optimisations (e.g.: >>1, jc for a tree-based decode).
If you have an architecture without either of those (say,
MIPS), then asm has little or no advantage over C.

>While C is usually the easier route, you should take such
>generalizations such "C is always easier than assembly"
>with a grain of salt.
>
>Here's a question for you kiddies, how in C would you
>implement a lightning fast "mod 96"? That is, optimize
>the following function:
>
>int MOD96(unsigned int x) {
> return x % 96;
>}
>
>Let's say you are also given that you know x is between
>0 and 2^24, from context. You're a little hard pressed
>given that its really only 1 C statement aren't you?

How's 'return (x-((unsigned int)x/96)*96);'?

It's not as clean, but it generates:
mov ecx, DWORD PTR _x$[esp-4]
mov eax, -1431655765 ; 0xaaaaaaab
mul ecx
shr edx, 6
lea eax, DWORD PTR [edx+edx*2]
shl eax, 5
mov edx, eax
mov eax, ecx
sub eax, edx


That's 17 Pentium clocks instead of your 15.
Damn. Writing it in asm could save a whole 11.7%!

>To see the answer in assembly see:
>
> http://www.pobox.com/~qed/adiv.html
>
>You aren't exactly going to duplicate that in an C encoding.

OK, it's not quite as neat, but the difference is minimal.
The divide is gone and the mul is the most significant part.


This bears out my point nicely, Paul. In asm you did the
it as divide/round/multiply/subtract, in C you did it as a
modulus. Compare like to like...

Let's just rub it in a bit further, shall we?
An algorithmic change, from % to /(int)*-, produces a 165%
speed-up. Coding that in asm produces a further 13%
speed-up.

Try this:
unsigned int div3(unsigned int x) {
return x/3;
}

VC5 makes that into:
mov eax, -1431655765 ;0xaaaaaaab
mul DWORD PTR _x$[esp-4]
mov eax, edx
shr eax, 1
ret

Try saving more than a single clock there...
I don't know why it decided to use 2/3, but I've seen it use
0x55555556 for /3, so I guess it's just some general
weirdness (perhaps that I'm not using SP3 at home)...

I don't know why VC5 does the divides, but not the mods,
however I'm sure it'll be included soon - after all, it's an
obvious extension...

>> > >when you need low-level access to the hardware, a
>> > >high level language tends to remove you from the
>> > >machine, into it's own world of abstractions.
>> >
>> > Really? C is a systems programming language. It has
>> > all the power of asm for pointer/memory handling. DOS
>> > C compilers have instructions to call interrupts and write
>> > to hardware ports. There is *nothing* I've seen done in
>> > asm that can't be done in C - the only difference is how
>> > much overhead gets added (which, IME, is not
>> > particularly significant due to the speed of the INT and
>> > IN/OUT instructions).
>>
>> Yeah, I agree that we don't need ASM to write device drivers.
>
>Well I don't! How do you write a MUTEX in C? You know
>that thing where you do an atom compare and exchange?
>(Look up cmpxchg, if you don't know what I am talking about.)
>
>What if you want to trap 16 bit and 32 bit interrupts and
>reroute them both to a particular bitness (i.e., a bimodal
>trap)? Admittedly this could be done with mostly C, and
>using the standard extensions in the Pmode APIs, but now
>lets say you wanted to trap *ALL 256* such interrupts. You
>either start running out of memory real fast, or you write
>some clever assembly to solve the problem.

And what's stopping you doing that with a couple of asm
routines or inline asm? Or generating the code with a
compiler and then adding your asm to the dump and
assembling it?

>> > This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the
>> > ability to try out new approaches and algorithms without
>> > getting bogged down in optimising the code and
>> > making it fit optimally with the rest of the code.
>
>C's only advantage here is that it may be a little faster,

A *little*?

>since you have all of C's built in assumptions that you can
>fall back on.

I don't know about you, but I find writing C a lot faster than
writing asm.

>The real advantage of C, in my opinion, is that it comes with
>standard libraries and uses a standardized calling
>conventions that are abstracted from the programming,
>which is for most of your coding is usually what you want.

Most compilers alter the calling convention from stack to
register calling when optimising, beating 'structured asm'
for performance.

>(It allows you to save the petal to the metal optimization
>effort for the places your profiler says you need it.)

Yup. That's a pretty major advantage, really.

>> Yes, agreed! Always prototype in C before committing
>> to ASM. If nothing else, you will have a debugged ASM
>> dump for your final ASM code.
>
>Well, we all have our methods for coding. Personally when
>I am tinkering around with little programs, I find myself look
>at Ralph Brown's interrupt list as much as I do C lib help
>files.

I don't think you can go far wrong with generating a 'reference
implementation' using the compiler and then looking for
places to optimise. Sometimes the compiler changes the
algorithm on you (switch-case->jump table, code->data
recursion), producing better results than you'd get by diving
straight into asm.

Russ

Brandon Van Every wrote in message
<01bd4f86$b06eb820$0d83...@hammurabi.blarg.net>...
>Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article
><889899583.24334.0...@news.demon.co.uk>...
>> Andreas Joos wrote in message ...
>> >
>> >Use a RISC-processor like the ARM-family (3DO, Newton,
>> >Acorn Archimedes/RiscPC) Small, easy to learn, efficient
>> >and powerfull instruction-/registerset.
>>
>> And almost no room for improvement over even a competent
>> compiler. Scheduling ops is a remarkably easy task with
>> lots of registers and a few, simple opcodes. In fact, I'd say
>> making the best use of 16/24/32/64/128 general registers is
>> far easier for a compiler than a human.
>
>Well you can say it, but that doesn't make it true. :-) How much actual
>hands-on experience do you have with these compilers, to back up your
>claim?
>
>I can't speak for ARM, but I know a lot about VC++ for DEC Alpha.
>Scheduling is pretty good for mathematical stuff. But, it is not tight
>enough to handle cases when you've run out of registers and you're clearing
>up calculations on-the-fly in a tight loop to make available space. e.g. a
>4x4 (matrix * vector) transform executing once every 16 cycles, where you
>don't want to re-load the matrix every time. In general, register capacity
>will always run out at some point, and the compiler will always choke at
>some point. It is in the nature of human speed-demons to push a CPU
>architecture to the limit, so this will always be a problem for any
>compiler and CPU.
>
>Also, there will be processing/scheduling tricks that seem easy enough to
>you as a human, but that the compiler writer wasn't planning on.
>Simultaneously processing (X) in both the FPU and the INT unit comes to
>mind. In 3d graphics you frequently do boolean ops on the integer
>representation of the FPU value to process stuff like trivial accept/reject
>and other boundary conditions. On Alpha there are architectural penalties
>for doing simultaneous float and int loads under certain conditions, and
>the compiler doesn't synchronize the scheduling well enough to prevent
>this. But hand-written ASM can do the job easily, you simply set up the
>computational dependencies the way you actually want them to align
>cycle-wise.
>
>Finally, the ARM is a "predicated" architecture, most of the instructions
>take an "if (X)" conditional as to whether they should be executed or not.
>At this point in our computational history, I doubt that compiler writers
>have spent a lot of time figuring out the potential scheduling benefits of
>such instructions.
I used the ARMcc compiler a couple of years back. In an attempt to optimise
the critical sections of the project, I thoroughly examined the asm output.
I think I managed to save about 3% each time by recoding or altering the
asm.
It wasn't worth it really. The ARMcc compiler seemed to generate pretty
clued-up
code. Of course I was somewhat less than an ARM guru, so I could have
missed some stuff. Still ithe ARM is not a complicated chip and I did read
the
manual :-)

I can only talk about 16 registers. But if I implement my routines bottom-up
in asm, I can make much better use of flags, registerusage, spare memory-
accesses and shifts, while the compiler have to do a top-down
implementation.

>>Get experience in ASM-programming, to get able to
>>out-optimising a compiler.
>

Optimising compiler produced asm-code is compareable to tuning a underpowered
car, while hand-writing efficient asm-code is more like a sport-car design/
production.

Of course, you can drive with a tuned VW beetle (Kaefer here in germany,
don't know how it's called outside), but I'd prefer a Ferrarie :)

>> Use processor-dependent data-structures and algorithmes,
>> you would NEVER use in C!

> Example?

Thinking about the really needed range of values, store them in a way
with the best mixture between size, non-sequental memory-accesses ...

Say if you have 3 24-bit values, 3 8bit-values and 1 32-bit-value
(coordinates of an object, in X,Y,Z, it's angles, in a,b,c
and a pointer to it's poligonlist) in 4 32-bitwords, on a quadword-
boundary on cpu with quadword-cachelinefills, you would reduce a lot
of cache-misses/flushes (on the ARM for example) if you would seperate
the 8bit-values out of the 32-bit words, that contains also the 24-
bitvalues.

What will a compiler of your choise compile out of the following:

(Checking if a coordinate is inside the visible screenarea in X-direction,
480x352 is used)

coordinate is a signed value!

IF coordinate <0 AND >479 THEN PROC_outside

'cause in Assembler there's no diffrence, I can use

CMP R0, #480
BHI outside ;every negativ number is higher, cause bit 31 is set...

>>Small Code.
>
> Most compilers can optimise for size.

I meaned a good mixture between size and speed, in requieremts for any
of your routines. A bit difficult to comile every routine with it's own
good/optimal options... that's one point, a human brain is far better.

It may be, that someone, who know a lot about the compiler and assembler,
can write c-code that will produce good code, but then, I can't see the big
advantage using c, when you have to look to special guidlines...

When I write something in Basic (most time fast prototyping or very small
tools (<200 lines), I think in other levels, compared to assembler...

mov edx,055555556h
mov ebx,eax
mul edx
and edx,0FFFFFFE0h
lea edx,[edx+edx*2]
sub ebx,edx

On the K6 yours takes 10 clocks, mine takes 7. It looks like 42% to me.



> >To see the answer in assembly see:
> >
> > http://www.pobox.com/~qed/adiv.html

> modulus. Compare like to like...
>

I'm not sure why you are saying this, it looks like matters are even
worse for C in this case.

> An algorithmic change, from % to /(int)*-, produces a 165%
> speed-up. Coding that in asm produces a further 13%
> speed-up.
>
> Try this:
> unsigned int div3(unsigned int x) {
> return x/3;
> }

As opposed to:

mov eax,05555556h
mul ebx
; result in edx.

4 clocks to your 6; 50%.



> Try saving more than a single clock there...
> I don't know why it decided to use 2/3, but I've seen it use
> 0x55555556 for /3, so I guess it's just some general
> weirdness (perhaps that I'm not using SP3 at home)...
>
> I don't know why VC5 does the divides, but not the mods,
> however I'm sure it'll be included soon - after all, it's an
> obvious extension...

No, the reason it doesn't do it is because it would be wrong. In the
case of "/3" it *is* numerically unstable. I suppose the folks at
Microsoft have worked out all the cases where it is, and where it isn't
which is why you see strange things like this. I assumed that they would
have seen the numerical instability the sometimes occurrs and decided not
to do it altogether.

Anyhow, part of the point I was trying to make was that the asm
programmer can ignore these things and just go for the faster set of
instructions, knowing the input range will be ok. With the C compiler,
your hands are tied, since they have to always produce correct results
for all inputs.

> >> > >when you need low-level access to the hardware, a high level language
> >> > >tends to remove you from the machine, into it's own world of
> >> > >abstractions.
> >> >
> >> > Really? C is a systems programming language. It has all the power of
> >> > asm for pointer/memory handling. DOS C compilers have instructions to
> >> > call interrupts and write to hardware ports. There is *nothing* I've
> >> > seen done in asm that can't be done in C - the only difference is how
> >> > much overhead gets added (which, IME, is not particularly significant
> >> > due to the speed of the INT and IN/OUT instructions).
> >>
> >> Yeah, I agree that we don't need ASM to write device drivers.
> >
> >Well I don't! How do you write a MUTEX in C? You know that thing where
> >you do an atom compare and exchange? (Look up cmpxchg, if you don't know
> >what I am talking about.)
> >
> >What if you want to trap 16 bit and 32 bit interrupts and reroute them
> >both to a particular bitness (i.e., a bimodal trap)? Admittedly this
> >could be done with mostly C, and using the standard extensions in the
> >Pmode APIs, but now lets say you wanted to trap *ALL 256* such interrupts.
> >You either start running out of memory real fast, or you write some
> >clever assembly to solve the problem.
>
> And what's stopping you doing that with a couple of asm routines or inline
> asm? Or generating the code with a compiler and then adding your asm to the
> dump and assembling it?

Nothing, in fact that's what I do. I'm not here to advocate "pure
assembly". Just a good working knowledge of it, so that you know where
to put it in your programs.



> >> > This is surely Cs greatest advantage over asm - the ability to
> >> > try out new approaches and algorithms without getting bogged down
> >> > in optimising the code and making it fit optimally with the rest
> >> > of the code.
> >
> >C's only advantage here is that it may be a little faster,
>
> A *little*?

If you have an asm frame work to start with, its not hard to just throw
together some code. Obviously, it depends on what you are starting with,
and the environment you want to test your code in. I learned about RDTSC
inside of assembly, but I taught myself FPU programming inside of a C
compiler (and a disassembler.)

> >since you have all of C's built in assumptions that you can fall back
> >on.
>
> I don't know about you, but I find writing C a lot faster than writing asm.

I find using a C compiler better mostly in that I can fall back on the
standard libraries, as I said. Printing stuff out and writing formatted
files is a major pain in ASM.

> >The real advantage of C, in my opinion, is that it comes with
> >standard libraries and uses a standardized calling conventions that
> >are abstracted from the programming, which is for most of your coding
> >is usually what you want.
>
> Most compilers alter the calling convention from stack to register
> calling when optimising, beating 'structured asm' for performance.

:o) You contradict yourself. "calling convention overhead" usually
doesn't show up as even a blip in anyone's performance profiles. At
least not if they are serious about performance.

> >(It allows you to save the petal to the metal optimization effort for
> >the places your profiler says you need it.)
>
> Yup. That's a pretty major advantage, really.
>
> >> Yes, agreed! Always prototype in C before committing to ASM. If
> >> nothing else, you will have a debugged ASM dump for your final ASM
> >> code.
> >
> >Well, we all have our methods for coding. Personally when
> >I am tinkering around with little programs, I find myself look
> >at Ralph Brown's interrupt list as much as I do C lib help
> >files.
>
> I don't think you can go far wrong with generating a 'reference
> implementation' using the compiler and then looking for places to
> optimise.

Well, the last time I did something like this I started with a C created
something horribly slow, then wrote ASM inner loops, then back ported to
a C reference model which was slightly faster that the first C model, but
not nearly as fast as the assembly. Want to try your C compiler on
something difficult:

int c_brot(double r, double i) {
double x,y,t,d;
int c;
c = 512;
x = r;
y = i;

d = 0.0;
do {
t = x*y;
x = x*x;
y = y*y;

if( d>4.0 ) goto bloopdone;
d = x+y;

x = x-y+r;
y = 2*t+i;
} while( (--c)>0 );

bloopdone:;

return c;
}

Its a rather famous inner loop. I'd be interested to see what VC came up
with. (Believe it or not, it is "prescheduled" for the Pentium, but this
won't help your poor C compiler.)

When you see what have to do just to approach the ASM language's
performance you will understand that having a knowledge of the underlying
asm is the only way you would think to try certain things.

> [...] Sometimes the compiler changes the algorithm on you

> (switch-case->jump table, code->data recursion), producing better results
> than you'd get by diving straight into asm.

The C compiler may beat you once ... but as the saying goes "fool me once
and you're the fool, fool me twice and I'm the fool".

--
Paul Hsieh
q...@pobox.com

>memory-accesses ...
>
>Say if you have 3 24-bit values, 3 8bit-values and 1
>32-bit-value (coordinates of an object, in X,Y,Z, it's angles,
>in a,b,c and a pointer to it's poligonlist) in 4 32-bitwords,
>on a quadword- boundary on cpu with quadword-cachelinefills,
>you would reduce a lot of cache-misses/flushes (on the ARM
>for example) if you would seperate the 8bit-values out of the
>32-bit words, that contains also the 24- bitvalues.

Surely that's just structure packing?
struct object {
union {
unsigned char a[4];
int x; }
union {
unsigned char b[4];
int y; }
union {
unsigned char c[4];
int z; }
void *ptr;
}

Is that what you meant? The alignment would be
automatic on most compilers. Of course, it would be nicer
as:

struct {
unsigned int xa, yb, zc;
void *ptr;
}

After all, to obtain x/y/z in the first structure, you'd need to
mask off the values. Using a shift and mask here for a/b/c
saves having to use anonymous unions which some
compilers choke on, or perhaps a pointer cast to grab a/b/c
as unsigned chars. Data structures are remarkably easy to
do in C...

>What will a compiler of your choise compile out of the following:

[...]


>IF coordinate <0 AND >479 THEN PROC_outside

Sean T Barrett answered this one just about perfectly.
There's nothing I can really add... That's just a remarkably
bad example if you want to show asm's 'superiority'.

>>>Small Code.
>>
>> Most compilers can optimise for size.
>
>I meaned a good mixture between size and speed, in
>requieremts for any of your routines. A bit difficult to comile
>every routine with it's own good/optimal options... that's
>one point, a human brain is far better.

Well, on most architectures, speed is roughly equal to
size, as far as I can tell. The good speed/size mixture can
usually be obtained by selecting 'maximise speed'. The
'minimise size' options are more suited to <64k executable
size challenges (assuming you remove the RTL and steal
the asm dump to assemble seperately). There's nothing
stopping you setting different size/speed options between
modules (or even between functions with #pragmas).

As for the brain being better, one word: Chess. Kasparov
may be able to beat most computers, but how many other
people would even stand a chance against Deep Blue?
Chess is just repeated application of a simple set of rules,
with some pre-defined sequences ('opening books').
Optimisation is just repeated application of a simple set
of rules, with some pre-defined sequences (e.g.: special
case handling on integer divides by constants).
The only thing that even gives you a chance is the brain's
ability to make intuitive leaps about what's going on.

>It may be, that someone, who know a lot about the compiler
>and assembler, can write c-code that will produce good
>code, but then, I can't see the big advantage using c, when
>you have to look to special guidlines...

Because writing C is (depending on the coder) 2-10x faster
than writing and optimising asm?

>When I write something in Basic (most time fast prototyping
>or very small tools (<200 lines), I think in other levels,
>compared to assembler...

And which do you find easiest to write code in? My guess
would be BASIC - you spend more time thinking about
what the code should do, rather than which opcode should
go where. C offers the same advantages, but good
compilers will get within 10-25% of optimal code, on
average.

>>>For example, on the Acorn RiscPC under RiscOS, a
>>>Hello-World binaery is 2 Instructions and 20 bytes long...
>>
>>>SWI "OS_WriteS": "Hello World"+CHR$(0): SWI "OS_Exit"
>>
>>>I've not found ONE compiler for RiscOS, that could do that.
>>
>> Well, I don't know about those compilers,
>
>GNU-C 2.7.x, for example...

Ah. GNU. Well known source of good optimisation - not!

>>>Say, if I'd like to write a game, that have to run on a
>>>133 Mhz Pentium, with 2 MB SVGA-Card at full speed,
>>>I don't have to code optimal pentium pro asm, 'cause
>>>there's no need to run it faster, then it's optimal speed.
>>
>> Problem: Given that it would be run on a multitasking
>> OS (Win95 - in a DOS box if necessary), we don't want
>> to waste cycles for other tasks. That would be rude.
>
>Problem: On what plattform is it more important to spare
>cycles for others tasks usage: For a 133 Mhz-Penitum or
>a PenitumPro 300 Mhz?
>
>So, if you compile it for PPro 300Mhz, it would give it's
>tasks some cycles more, but on the Penitum it would
>give less...

On which of those is it OK to write sub-optimal assembly?
If you don't need the speed, don't use asm. The compiler
will optimise as best it can, and will likely make it run better
on both platforms.

>Another question, 'cause don't play on modern PCs (just
>an old 486SLX- Emulator for DOS and WIN :), just right
>for Settlers, Simon the Sorccerer etc.):
>
>How much games do really run in multitasking, even if
>possible, doesn't most gamers run it in fullscreen?

Full screen != Exclusive. A lot of people like to do things in
the background - compiling, downloading, running Office 97,
reading news, virus checkers, Catz, etc.

PS - Would everyone reading this and writing a Windows
game please make sure they look up and use WaitMessage()
when their game is minimised? You really don't need 99%
CPU time when you're in the background... Thankyou.

>>>As long, as the module/objects and it's interfaces etc.
>>>between them are well defined before...
>>
>> Why? I mean, sure, it's easier that way, but interfaces are
>> no more difficult to change than anything else.
>
>If the specification/defination-phase is ready, no more

>changes should be made. Only in very few cases there


>should be a reentry in this phase, when you're already in
>the implementation-phase...

I've yet to meet anyone who can make a game fun purely
from the design stage. Games need playing, and honing,
not being handed down from on high by the designer.

>> Of course. That's because pipelined, stall-free asm is
>> *so* easy to generate?
>
>Are there no assemblers/tools avaiable, that could
>rearange your asm-code? :)

Doubtful. Optimising assemblers were essentially killed
by optimising compilers.

>> Removing ONE instruction from a medium-sized routine
>> of Pentium asm is likely to mean at least half an hour's
>> work re-pairing the ops and making sure no stalls
>> appear.
>
>I believe, that x86-assembler is ugly :)

It's powerful, though. RISC asm is boring - there's no room
for cool tricks. If you consider a C derived RISC architecture,
like MIPS, then there are probably no flags - C doesn't do
carry flags. That rules out all those cool multi-precision,
bit mangling and fixed-point tricks.

Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article
<889972649.23633.0...@news.demon.co.uk>...

>
> A Huffman coder is probably an ideal application for
> asm - you get the double-precision shifts and carry
> optimisations (e.g.: >>1, jc for a tree-based decode).
> If you have an architecture without either of those (say,
> MIPS), then asm has little or no advantage over C.
Use ASM when it is advantageous. Of course it's not going to be
advantageous in many cases. Or, it may not offer more than a 10% advantage
and the development time may not be justified. But thoroughly knowing ASM
for your CPU architecture gives you another way to think about and attack
problems. Who cares about Huffman coders, there's going to be some unknown
"algorithm X" that you can put to good advantage in ASM if you think about
your problems in that way.

[code snippet removed]

> That's 17 Pentium clocks instead of your 15.
> Damn. Writing it in asm could save a whole 11.7%!

Which when you're trying to win an OpenGL Viewperf benchmark, is
everything. You never said anything about how important that 11.7% was to
somebody, did you? Often it doesn't matter at all. But sometimes, it
matters very very much. It all depends on who's attention is focused
where, and how many $$$$$$ are in their pocket.

> Let's just rub it in a bit further, shall we?
> An algorithmic change, from % to /(int)*-, produces a 165%
> speed-up. Coding that in asm produces a further 13%
> speed-up.

In benchmarking, marketers need every piece of ammo they can get their
hands on. Those stupid bar charts in Ziff-Davis can make or break a
product, even if it's only so much real-world baloney. If it takes 1 month
to redesign the algorithm and then an extra 1 week to write the ASM version
to get that last 13%, then it can be worth it. Don't sneeze at incremental
performance improvements, if you can get them to add up, the effect is
powerful. (The real problem is you can't always get them to add up. :-(

> I don't know why VC5 does the divides, but not the mods,
> however I'm sure it'll be included soon - after all, it's an
> obvious extension...

That sounds like an academic "Any Good Compiler" argument. As you said
yourself, that's not the real world.

> >> Yeah, I agree that we don't need ASM to write device drivers.
> >
> >Well I don't! How do you write a MUTEX in C?

You call an OS primitive to do it for you. Yes someone needed ASM to do
the mutex primitive in the first place, but the driver writer doesn't need
to reinvent the wheel.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Andy Buchanan <o...@clara.net> wrote in article
<6eh1qg$gvo$1...@eros.clara.net>...

>
> I used the ARMcc compiler a couple of years back. In an attempt to
optimise
> the critical sections of the project, I thoroughly examined the asm
output.
> I think I managed to save about 3% each time by recoding or altering the
> asm.
> It wasn't worth it really. The ARMcc compiler seemed to generate pretty
> clued-up
> code. Of course I was somewhat less than an ARM guru, so I could have
> missed some stuff. Still ithe ARM is not a complicated chip and I did
read
> the
> manual :-)
The first Alpha ASM code I wrote gained me exactly 0% improvement in the
code I was working on. The code was cache memory bound and the compiler
was plenty good at optimizing the add-multiply sequences. I was simply
barking up the wrong tree. A year later when I was much more experienced,
the best Alpha ASM code I wrote was for a matrix transform loop that
improved from 2M (matrix*vectors)/sec to 7M in the real world, and hit the
peak 20M for small in-cache datasets. No way the compiler could have ever
generated that, it was a heavy-duty scheduling issue not an algorithmic
issue. Unfortunately it didn't improve our driver quite so dramatically
because of the 128-byte vertex stride (a Microsoft MCD design-ism), I got
maybe a 5%..10% improvement in driver speed overall. A few months ago I
worked on some routine that took our driver from 400K triangles/sec to
500K. I re-ordered a divide and some clip tests in C, then improved the
scheduling in ASM. The C took 1 day and was worth 80% of the benefit. The
ASM took 2 days and was 20% of the benefit. Given the overall improvement,
the effort on the ASM was definitely worth it.

So, the moral of the story is that with experience one gets better results,
and one knows more about where to spend the energy.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Or fall apart, because the car wasn't originally built to handle that much
engine vibration? All I'm really saying is that these metaphors can lead
away from the subject matter....

> >Problem: On what plattform is it more important to spare
> >cycles for others tasks usage: For a 133 Mhz-Penitum or
> >a PenitumPro 300 Mhz?

This is a marketing question, not a technical one. Today in the
animation/CAD world, it is obviously better to write for the Pentium II 300
MHz.

> >I believe, that x86-assembler is ugly :)
>
> It's powerful, though. RISC asm is boring - there's no room
> for cool tricks. If you consider a C derived RISC architecture,
> like MIPS, then there are probably no flags - C doesn't do
> carry flags. That rules out all those cool multi-precision,
> bit mangling and fixed-point tricks.
>
> ---
> Russ

Russ, all I can say from your comment above is that you *clearly* have no
experience with a recent RISC architecture like DEC Alpha EV5 or MIPS
R10000. Quad issue pipelines with 1 FPU add and 1 FPU multiply are very
cool, much cooler than X86 architecture will ever be. Alpha's max
theoretical 4x4 (matrix*vector) is 16 cycles/iteration, whereas for Pentium
II it's about 40 cycles/iteration. But X86 is where the money is, so
that's where I'm going now....

As for flags, RISC architectures don't have them, so that they don't have
to introduce a dependency between setting a flag and acting upon the
results of the flag. On Alpha I can get oodles of comparisons done without
having to worry about whether some producer-consumer dependency would
de-rail the whole show.

I think I've said all I have to say for now. In closing, it's all subject
to the classic engineering answer: "It Depends." And on that note,


E.O.T.
Brandon Van Every

Andreas Joos wrote:
>
> In message <889899583.24334.0...@news.demon.co.uk>
> "Russ Williams" <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote:

> >>Use a RISC-processor like the ARM-family (3DO, Newton,
> >>Acorn Archimedes/RiscPC) Small, easy to learn, efficient
> >>and powerfull instruction-/registerset.
> >
> > And almost no room for improvement over even a competent
> > compiler. Scheduling ops is a remarkably easy task with
> > lots of registers and a few, simple opcodes. In fact, I'd say
> > making the best use of 16/24/32/64/128 general registers is
> > far easier for a compiler than a human.
>
> I can only talk about 16 registers. But if I implement my routines bottom-up
> in asm, I can make much better use of flags, registerusage, spare memory-
> accesses and shifts, while the compiler have to do a top-down
> implementation.
If you're programming in 16-bit (real) mode then you're not making
ANYTHING LIKE the machine's full use anyway. Modern CPUs (i.e. the
Pentium on) are optimised for 32-bit protected mode, which is a much
better memory model anyway. FWIW using 32-bit regs in 16-bit mode or
vice-versa causes hits on P5 or above (because of the prefix)
[skip the clumsy simile discussed in other replies]


> Say if you have 3 24-bit values, 3 8bit-values and 1 32-bit-value
> (coordinates of an object, in X,Y,Z, it's angles, in a,b,c
> and a pointer to it's poligonlist) in 4 32-bitwords, on a quadword-
> boundary on cpu with quadword-cachelinefills, you would reduce a lot
> of cache-misses/flushes (on the ARM for example) if you would seperate
> the 8bit-values out of the 32-bit words, that contains also the 24-
> bitvalues.

What's your point here? About alignment? Telling me compilers can't
align?

> What will a compiler of your choise compile out of the following:
>
> (Checking if a coordinate is inside the visible screenarea in X-direction,
> 480x352 is used)
>
> coordinate is a signed value!
>
> IF coordinate <0 AND >479 THEN PROC_outside
>
> 'cause in Assembler there's no diffrence, I can use
>

> BHI outside ;every negativ number is higher, cause bit 31 is set...

This is dealt with in another thread.

> >>Small Code.
> >
> > Most compilers can optimise for size.
>
> I meaned a good mixture between size and speed, in requieremts for any
> of your routines. A bit difficult to comile every routine with it's own
> good/optimal options... that's one point, a human brain is far better.

But optimising compilers are designd by human brains, and will try to
apply many tricks (common or not) to code, and will also take into
account the MANY complexities of, say, the 5th gen+ x86 CPUs. Pairing by
hand throughtout code, for example, is a nightmare for any more than a
small ASM section.

> It may be, that someone, who know a lot about the compiler and assembler,
> can write c-code that will produce good code, but then, I can't see the big
> advantage using c, when you have to look to special guidlines...

Because it takes 1/10th of the time to develop, and you can concentrate
on optimising those inner loops rather than other sections. Plus, if the
other section get 90%+ of possible speed, why bother?

to

Paul Hsieh <q...@pobox.com> wrote:
>On Sun, 15 Mar 1998 14:35:30 -0000, ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk says...
>> How's 'return (x-((unsigned int)x/96)*96);'?
>
>Hmmm ... I did not realize the VC performed this optimization; I thought
>it was numerically unstable, but it appears to depend highly on the
>scaled number they chose.
The point, of course, is that compilers can optimize.
They are at their best with this sort of thing: what
is the best code sequence with which to generate a
single C operation. Compilers are good at that
because a human programmer makes that call. They
get less good at things like scheduling and what not.
(The old MIPS compiler that did inter-procedural
register allocation notwithstanding.)

>On the K6 yours takes 10 clocks, mine takes 7. It looks like 42% to me.

And what if it gets scheduled in with other code?
Besides which, perhaps you should compare that
with a compiler design to produced optimized K6
code! I mean, I'm not going to blame it on MSVC
if their generated code is suboptimal on the x86
emulator running on my powerPC, either.

Ok, maybe there's no K6-optimizing compilers (umm,
but that would say something about the K6 to me).
If there were, however, there's a fair chance
you would get better code by optimizing once for
each target platform. Of _course_ you can always
do at least as well (and probably better) if you
do it by hand, but most people do not have infinite
time.

(I noticed I don't see you listing PPro timings.
Why'd you pick the K6?)

>No, the reason it doesn't do it is because it would be wrong. In the
>case of "/3" it *is* numerically unstable. I suppose the folks at
>Microsoft have worked out all the cases where it is, and where it isn't
>which is why you see strange things like this. I assumed that they would
>have seen the numerical instability the sometimes occurrs and decided not
>to do it altogether.
>
>Anyhow, part of the point I was trying to make was that the asm
>programmer can ignore these things and just go for the faster set of
>instructions, knowing the input range will be ok. With the C compiler,
>your hands are tied, since they have to always produce correct results
>for all inputs.

Sure, but this doesn't mean a compiler couldn't do it.
You just need to communicate the extra information
to the compiler:

if ((unsigned) x < (1 << 24)) // or whatever your range
return x/3;
else
return 0;

Of course, there's very little chance any compiler
will actually optimize this case appropriately; but
there's 0 chance when you don't provide all the information
to the compiler. (I have a criticism of C here--I want
to be able to use a compiler-based assert statement
to get the above with no branch:

assert((unsigned) x < (1 << 24));
return x/3;

That would be very useful for eliminating the range
check in a switch statement, which is the main case
I'd expect them to make use of it.)

But, if this idiom were common, you would expect
the compiler to do it. And for the idioms the
compiler is familiar with, it will _always_ produce
the "optimal" code... as opposed to when you try to
do the above (x/3) (for _all_ x) and decide that it's
numerically unstable and can't be done... looks like
the compiler beat you on that one, huh?

>Nothing, in fact that's what I do. I'm not here to advocate "pure
>assembly". Just a good working knowledge of it, so that you know where
>to put it in your programs.

The problem is that every time somebody posts
a case where ASM is faster, a faction starts
up more posts about how ASM is better, and the
whole pure-ASM vs. C-plus-ASM flamewares start
up again. I think there is maybe _one_ poster
who has seriously advocated 0% assembly.

Can we somehow turn this thread constructive?

How about "the complete list of reasons why you,
the programmer who doesn't mind using C/C++,
might want to use ASM for only a 10% gain":

- speed critical routine in an app (as reported
by a profiler)

- library or driver (especially if it is going
to compete with others on performance benchmarks)

"The complete list of reasons when ASM might
buy you _significantly_ more than 10%":

- very very short inner loops where 2 instructions
might be 20-30% of execution time

- optimizing a routine for a target not directly
supported by the optimizer (e.g. K6)

- using techniques not readily available from C:
- carry bits
- multi-word results (multiplication/divide)
- partial register manipulation

"Other special cases where asm is necessary"

- writing a compiler

- writing core library/OS primitives (e.g. mutex)

Obviously these lists are incomplete at
this time. Anybody think this would make
a useful format for a "middle-of-the-road"
advocacy FAQ on ASM vs. C?

Sean

to

Brandon Van Every <vane...@blarg.net> wrote:
>> if ((unsigned) x >= 480)

>But would people know to do this, if they didn't have a thorough knowledge
>of ASM in the first place? I'm all for making C code produce good ASM
>code. But you have to know what good ASM code is, for that to happen.
Response #1: As I said, in this particular case,
the _C compiler_ already has the appropriate knowledge.

Response #2: No, you just have to know that your
C implementation uses 2's complement

Response #3: No, you just have to know the C spec.
This is well-defined on all C implementations, so
why would you have to know any ASM? (The point of
response #2 is that even if you don't know the spec,
you can figure it out for yourself.)

Certainly you _can_ figure this out by saying
to yourself "how do I get the compiler to generate
the right kind of compare", but that's not the only
way.

For reference on #3:

"Standard C: A reference", Plauger and Brodie, p. 91,
Table 7.2, conversion from signed int to unsigned int
when N==M (the types are the same size):

if 0 <= X then X else X+2^N

Of course, on a non-two's-complement machine,
this means the cast of (unsigned) will not be
a no-op. This simply argues "you need to know
how expensive each primitive operation in your
implementation is", not that you need to know
any ASM.[*]

In closing, I will note that this optimization
and the related if ((unsigned) (x-a) >= (b-a))
are well known in the literature, and I've seen
them written much more often in C or C-style
pseudocode than in ASM. So I especially think
you don't need ASM to do this one. You just
need to read about it somewhere, or else think
about the representation of datatypes on that
implementation.

Sean
[*] of course, looking at the ASM is the easiest
way of understanding the costs on an unfamiliar
compiler; I personally advocate learning ASM if
you're going to be seriously optimizing code,
but I'm also used to working on teams of six
programmers where only one of them needs that
knowledge--the rest just need to know enough
about optimization in general to avoid using
O(n^2) algorithms and the like.

removed. It's the floating-point divides per pixel that you need
to watch for.

>> architecture, like MIPS, then there are probably no
>> flags - C doesn't do carry flags. That rules out all those
>> cool multi-precision, bit mangling and fixed-point tricks.
>
>Russ, all I can say from your comment above is that you
>*clearly* have no experience with a recent RISC
>architecture like DEC Alpha EV5 or MIPS R10000.
Nope, as I've stated several times in this thread, my RISC
experience is with the R3000.

>Quad issue pipelines with 1 FPU add and 1 FPU multiply
>are very cool, much cooler than X86 architecture will ever
>be.

I still say the code is boring.

x86 is pathetic as far as architectures go, but it also offers
lots of room for optimisation tricks.

How do you figure? The change from a true modulus to this
method produces an improvement over an order of magnitude
greater than going from VC5 to asm. I'd say that fairly nicely
bears out the 'algorithmic changes are better than going to

>

>usually doesn't show up as even a blip in anyone's
>performance profiles. At least not if they are serious about

Of course - but it is symptomatic of the type of things compilers
do for you. You get a nice clean way to write code and call
functions and the compiler then goes away and tries to make it
faster. Asm doesn't give yout that choice - it's either fast or
clean, IME.

[...]


>Want to try your C compiler on something difficult:

Sure, why not?

>int c_brot(double r, double i) {
>double x,y,t,d;
>int c;
> c = 512;
> x = r;
> y = i;
>
> d = 0.0;
> do {
> t = x*y;
> x = x*x;
> y = y*y;
>
> if( d>4.0 ) goto bloopdone;
> d = x+y;
>
> x = x-y+r;
> y = 2*t+i;
> } while( (--c)>0 );
>
>bloopdone:;
>
> return c;
>}

push ebp
mov ebp, esp
and esp, -8 ; fffffff8H
sub esp, 16 ; 00000010H
; Line 11
fld QWORD PTR _r$[ebp]
fld QWORD PTR _i$[ebp]
fld ST(0)
fld ST(2)
; Line 12
fld ST(2)
; Line 23
fxch ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(4)
fxch ST(1)
fmul ST(0), ST(4)
fxch ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(3)
fxch ST(1)
fxch ST(2)
fxch ST(1)
mov ecx, 512 ; 00000200H
$L90:
fld ST(1)
fsub ST(0), ST(1)
fxch ST(3)
fmul QWORD PTR $T101
fxch ST(1)
fadd ST(0), ST(2)
fxch ST(3)
fadd ST(0), ST(5)
fxch ST(1)
fsubr ST(0), ST(4)
fxch ST(1)
dec ecx
test ecx, ecx
fstp QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
fstp QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
fstp ST(0)
jle SHORT $L103
fcomp QWORD PTR $T102
fld QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
fld QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
fld QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
fxch ST(2)
fmul QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
fxch ST(1)
fmul QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
fxch ST(2)
fmul QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
fxch ST(1)
fxch ST(2)
fxch ST(1)
fnstsw ax
test ah, 65 ; 00000041H
jne SHORT $L90
fstp ST(0)
fstp ST(0)
$L103:
fstp ST(0)
fstp ST(0)
fstp ST(0)
mov eax, ecx
; Line 24
mov esp, ebp
pop ebp
ret 0

>Its a rather famous inner loop. I'd be interested to see what
>VC came up with. (Believe it or not, it is "prescheduled" for
>the Pentium, but this won't help your poor C compiler.)

OK, so how'd it do? I'm not familiar enough with FPU code
to read it properly... It seems to be doing a lot of fxchs, so I'd
guess the FPU isn't waiting around too much.

buz...@world.std.com said:

> Paul Hsieh <q...@pobox.com> wrote:
> >On Sun, 15 Mar 1998 14:35:30 -0000, ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk says...
> >> How's 'return (x-((unsigned int)x/96)*96);'?
> >
> >Hmmm ... I did not realize the VC performed this optimization; I thought
> >it was numerically unstable, but it appears to depend highly on the
> >scaled number they chose.
>
> The point, of course, is that compilers can optimize. They are at their
> best with this sort of thing: what is the best code sequence with which
> to generate a single C operation.
What is the relevant of a *single* C operation? I personally am more
interested in optimal encodings of "algorithmic primitives." You will
notice the C compiler was shy by three clocks against my implementation, for
two reasons (1) The code generated for one of the clocks was simply a
weakness in the compiler (less than optimal simulated multiply) (2) The
other two lost clocks lost were lost because of the constraints of the "C
language" required a 100% correct result, when a slight compromise to the
correctness still was within the algorithmic requirements.

(Either way, I was impressed that VC++ was able to come within 3 clocks of
my code.)

> Compilers are good at that because a human programmer makes that call.
> They get less good at things like scheduling and what not. (The old MIPS
> compiler that did inter-procedural register allocation notwithstanding.)
>

> >On the K6 yours takes 10 clocks, mine takes 7. It looks like 42% to me.
>

> And what if it gets scheduled in with other code?

My recollection of the code was that all cycles and slots were occupied.
(Besides innocuous things that have the expected behavior, such as FPU
activity)

> Besides which, perhaps you should compare that with a compiler design to
> produced optimized K6 code!

My code is simultaneously optimal for Pentium, K6, and the PPro (as it
turns out.)

> [...] I mean, I'm not going to blame it on MSVC if their generated code

> is suboptimal on the x86 emulator running on my powerPC, either.

Huh? You'll excuse me if I ignore optimization for platforms such as "x86
emulators on degenerate platforms".



> Ok, maybe there's no K6-optimizing compilers (umm, but that would say
> something about the K6 to me).

The K6 is a rather remarkable CPU that is naturally optimal under more
conditions that Intel CPUs. I have only ever written one piece of code
optimized for the K6 in my life, and that code wasn't it. (The code from
the snippet you deleted was written a long time ago optimized for Pentiums.)

> [...] If there were, however, there's a fair chance you would get better

> code by optimizing once for each target platform. Of _course_ you can
> always do at least as well (and probably better) if you do it by hand,
> but most people do not have infinite time.

Oh boy, its so much for to have words being put in my mouth. What's wrong
with all you people? I never said, nor do I endorse the idea of writing
code totally in assembly instead of C/ASM mixtures.

> [...] (I noticed I don't see you listing PPro timings. Why'd you pick the
> K6?)

I didn't have the PPro timings handy, but with the K6, its trivial to
memorize nearly all the rules. Working out the exact performance on the
PPro is exceedingly difficult. Its funny that the K6 and PPro are designed
on very similar principles, yet one is easy to eyeball the performance on,
they other is nearly impossible.

> >No, the reason it doesn't do it is because it would be wrong. In the
> >case of "/3" it *is* numerically unstable. I suppose the folks at
> >Microsoft have worked out all the cases where it is, and where it isn't
> >which is why you see strange things like this. I assumed that they would
> >have seen the numerical instability the sometimes occurrs and decided not
> >to do it altogether.
> >
> >Anyhow, part of the point I was trying to make was that the asm
> >programmer can ignore these things and just go for the faster set of
> >instructions, knowing the input range will be ok. With the C compiler,
> >your hands are tied, since they have to always produce correct results
> >for all inputs.
>

> Sure, but this doesn't mean a compiler couldn't do it.
> You just need to communicate the extra information
> to the compiler:
>
> if ((unsigned) x < (1 << 24)) // or whatever your range
> return x/3;
> else
> return 0;

Uhh ... I don't see your point, this just makes the code slower, and I defy
*any* C compiler to realize that it can use the numerically unstable code
for the non-zero return.

> Of course, there's very little chance any compiler will actually optimize
> this case appropriately; but there's 0 chance when you don't provide all
> the information to the compiler. (I have a criticism of C here--I want
> to be able to use a compiler-based assert statement to get the above with
> no branch:
>
> assert((unsigned) x < (1 << 24));
> return x/3;

Except that 'assert' may be redefined at link time, so this isn't going to
help either. If you want to extend the C language I would prefer:

assume( in(x,(0,(1<<24))) );

Either way, you are going to have a problem motivating C compiler vendors to
work real hard on this.

> That would be very useful for eliminating the range check in a switch
> statement, which is the main case I'd expect them to make use of it.)
>
> But, if this idiom were common, you would expect the compiler to do it.
> And for the idioms the compiler is familiar with, it will _always_ produce
> the "optimal" code... as opposed to when you try to do the above (x/3)
> (for _all_ x) and decide that it's numerically unstable and can't be
> done... looks like the compiler beat you on that one, huh?

No such thing occurred, since I have never tried to beat the compiler on
that problem. Had I been interested in solving that problem, I would have
continued to search for a better solution. Kepp in mind that its still not
100% clear that the C compiler came up with the best code (I did beat it by
2 clocks after all.)

> >Nothing, in fact that's what I do. I'm not here to advocate "pure
> >assembly". Just a good working knowledge of it, so that you know where
> >to put it in your programs.
>

> The problem is that every time somebody posts a case where ASM is faster,

> whole pure-ASM vs. C-plus-ASM flamewares start up again. I think there

But its never quite that simple. I watch people say "the C compiler will
come withing 10% anyways ...", "only the very best assembly programmers can
beat compilers" and "assembly takes so much longer" with the natural
assumption that anyone advocating a more critical approach is a "pure asm
advocate".

> Can we somehow turn this thread constructive?
>
> How about "the complete list of reasons why you, the programmer who
> doesn't mind using C/C++, might want to use ASM for only a 10% gain":

Anthony D. Tribelli's profile photo
Anthony D. Tribelli
unread,
Mar 17, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Russ Williams (ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk) wrote:
: Wim Libaers wrote in message <6e9fo0$o0j$3...@news1.skynet.be>...
: >2. Some things are easier in asm:
:
: Like what?

One simple example is 16.16 fixed point multiply. Two 32-bit operands and
a 64-bit result that gets shifted right 16-bits to get back into range.
On x86 it takes one extra assembly instruction compared to a regular
32-bit multiply. This sort of thing can't really be done with comparable
efficiency on x86 CPUs in C. Casting the multiply operands to compiler
specific 64-bit types produced calls to a 64-bit math library last time I
checked (VC++ 4.2).

Tony

Tony Tribelli

Ok I broke form to follow up a few things very briefly....
Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article

<6ebq07$4ca$1...@flex.news.pipex.net>...
>
> But does anyone *need* to know C++ in it's entirety?

Doubtful. :-)

> (For that matter *does* anyone know C++ in it's entirety?)

I used to. I don't anymore. Things have changed in 2 years and I've been
doing drivers in C.

> >> If you only know a couple of instructions then you most
> >> definitely should NOT be using asm. What is the point
> >> of using asm if you aren't capable of out-optimising a
> >> compiler?
> >
> >Heard of RISC?
>

> Yes. I guess there's a fine point with RISC, but this thread
> is about x86. How could RISC asm be the language of
> choice when the dominant platform is CRISC?

Pentium Pro/II is a RISC chip with an ugly X86 interface thown over it.

> >You still have to know about cycle counts to make the C
> >code go fast.
>

> Not specifically. You just need to know things like:
> * indirecting through a pointer is slower than direct
> addressing, but faster than copying the data.

Depends on the problem and cache hierarchy characteristics. Although this
is a higher-level design issue, not a C vs. ASM problem per se. One day
we'll have ASM instructions to control memory caches at the user level, but
don't hold your breath. :-)

> * function pointers are slower than function calls

But if you can afford the one then you can afford the other. If you
shouldn't be affording the one, then you shouldn't be affording the other.

> * multiply/divide slow,

Divide slow. FPU multiply fast even since i486 days. INT multiply slow.
If you know some contemporary ASM then you know this. It is a common
mistake as a beginner in 3d graphics to devise algorithms based on the CPU
assumptions of yesteryear, when a given article or book was written.
Bresenham if..then algorithms and categorically avoiding a 1/z divide
spring to mind.

> shifts/add/subtract/logic fast

Sure.

> * ifs/cases bad, jump tables good

Why? For what?

> Those rules apply to probably every CPU in existence
> to varying degrees.

Didn't you just write some kind of meta-oxymoron or content-free statement
here? :-)

> It doesn't tell you the specifics of
> the CPU, but fast C tends to stay fast when ported.

ASM is about specifics. My point is that all of the above issues would be
decisively resolved if you had a thorough knowledge of your CPU's ASM. And
lacking that knowlege, one makes mistakes. How do you write your C
algorithms when a Pentium doesn't allow simultaneous INT and FPU pairing,
but a Pentium Pro/II does?

> Pure dumb luck is an option, too. A good compiler and an
> idiosyncratic bit of coding can get near-asm speeds without
> the coder having the faintest idea why. Naturally, I wouldn't
> recommend this...

I would! Who gives a damn why you've gotten it to go faster, if you can
verify that you actually got it to go faster? Often the optimization
search space is too large for you to code up all the options within the
time you have to complete the assignment, so if it works use it. If you're
lost and you keep twiddling, you could very well make it slower again and
be none the wiser for it....


Cheetohs,
Brandon Van Every
