Brandon Van Every's profile photo

to

Sean T Barrett <buz...@world.std.com> wrote in article
<EpwwL...@world.std.com>...

> Brandon Van Every <vane...@blarg.net> wrote:
> >> if ((unsigned) x >= 480)
> >But would people know to do this, if they didn't have a thorough
knowledge
> >of ASM in the first place? I'm all for making C code produce good ASM
> >code. But you have to know what good ASM code is, for that to happen.
>
> Response #1: As I said, in this particular case,
> the _C compiler_ already has the appropriate knowledge.
Sean, your optimization was much deeper than what I was referring to. Sure
the compiler has the knowledge, but how does the programmer even know
they're supposed to use a constant to speed something up??!? Even an
apparently stupid question like this ultimately requires some ASM knowledge
for a good answer. And in any event, how large a constant, and where is it
stored? On Alpha architecture, (x >= 255) is faster than (x >= 65535) is
faster than (x >= 27565535). You wouldn't know that unless you knew about
8-bit literal encodings vs. 16-bit address offset encodings vs. plain old
register loads from program data space. On Intel architecture, register
poverty can force you to load the constant from memory at every pass
through the loop. Depending on the loop, is this better/worse than doing
extra computational work?


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

you get from assembly language. You understand that such a stance is not a
defence of the actual C language itself, only the point that you shouldn't
code like a putz. Either way, you aren't going to be able to even follow
what is going on here if you don't have some basic knowledge of assembly.

> >> An algorithmic change, from % to /(int)*-, produces a 165% speed-up.
> >> Coding that in asm produces a further 13% speed-up.
> >>
> >> Try this:
> >> unsigned int div3(unsigned int x) {
> >> return x/3;
> >> }
> >>
> >> VC5 makes that into:
> >> mov eax, -1431655765 ;0xaaaaaaab
> >> mul DWORD PTR _x$[esp-4]
> >> mov eax, edx
> >> shr eax, 1
> >> ret
> >
> >As opposed to:
> >
> > mov eax,05555556h
> > mul ebx
> >; result in edx.
> >
> >4 clocks to your 6; 50%.
>
> Since when has edx been standard for return values?

"standard return values"?? What kind of piece of crap compiler are you --
oh yeah, I forgot, MSVC imposes this arbitrary restriction for no good
reason. Sorry buddy, but I'm afraid MS is a little out numbered on this
one. WATCOM C/C++ and DJGPP have been supporting arbitrary return and
parameter register for static functions since their inception. That is to
say, the very first 32 bit C compilers available for the PC have supported
this all along.

> [...] - but it is symptomatic of the type of things compilers


> do for you. You get a nice clean way to write code and call
> functions and the compiler then goes away and tries to make it
> faster. Asm doesn't give yout that choice - it's either fast or
> clean, IME.

Fast *and* clean are not necessarily at cross purposes, IME.

> [...]
> >Want to try your C compiler on something difficult:
>
> Sure, why not?

:o) Oh brother ... Poor MSVC isn't doing very well on this one. The loop I
have in mind, which appears on my page at:

http://www.pobox.com/~qed/p5opt.html

runs in 15 clocks.

The inner loop ...

> $L90:
+1 brt
u x > fld ST(1)
u > fsub ST(0), ST(1)
v > fxch ST(3)
u > fmul QWORD PTR $T101
v > fxch ST(1)
u > fadd ST(0), ST(2)
v > fxch ST(3)
u > fadd ST(0), ST(5)
v > fxch ST(1)
u x > fsubr ST(0), ST(4)
x v > fxch ST(1)
u > dec ecx
v > test ecx, ecx
u x > fstp QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
u x > fstp QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
u x > fstp ST(0)
x v > jle SHORT $L103
u x > fcomp QWORD PTR $T102
u x > fld QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
u x > fld QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
u > fld QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
v > fxch ST(2)
u > fmul QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
v > fxch ST(1)
+1 FMUL stall
u > fmul QWORD PTR _x$[esp+16]
v > fxch ST(2)
+1 FMUL stall
u > fmul QWORD PTR _y$[esp+16]
v > fxch ST(1)
x v > fxch ST(2)
x v > fxch ST(1)
+6 > fnstsw ax
u > test ah, 65 ; 00000041H
v > jne SHORT $L90

> fstp ST(0)
> fstp ST(0)
> $L103:
> fstp ST(0)
> fstp ST(0)
> fstp ST(0)
> mov eax, ecx
> ; Line 24
> mov esp, ebp
> pop ebp
> ret 0
>
> >Its a rather famous inner loop. I'd be interested to see what
> >VC came up with. (Believe it or not, it is "prescheduled" for
> >the Pentium, but this won't help your poor C compiler.)
>
> OK, so how'd it do?

31 clocks for the inner loop. I first learned how to optimize FPU code on
this loop. On my very first attempt I managed 20 clocks. As I indicated,
the best loop I've seen so far is 15 clocks. Of course you missed your
opportunity to prove that algorithmic considerations are more important,
since there are ways to unroll this loop to improve performance. (I think
in theory its possible to reach an asymptotic performance of 10 clocks ...)

Anyhow, I used Pentium clocks, simply because the K6 has a deficient FPU.
Nevertheless, my hand optimized K6 loop (yes, this is the *one* piece of
code I've optimized for the K6) out performs your loop on a Pentium (I think
its about 25 clocks; your loop would be *exceedingly* slow on a K6, about 50
clocks as a quick estimate.)

> [...] I'm not familiar enough with FPU code to read it properly... It

> seems to be doing a lot of fxchs, so I'd guess the FPU isn't waiting
> around too much.

No, the MSVC compiler does seem to know how to avoid data dependencies.

However it missed 2 FMUL stalls, has 3 unpaired FXCH's, has a FSTP ST(0) on
the *INNER LOOP* (you can almost always slap a "P" on the end of some other
FPU instruction to accomplish the same thing for free), is reading/writing
parameters instead of doing FPU work with 6 instructions (vs. 2 in Damien
Jones' loop), and of course, it has missed the positive IEEE numeric compare
trick.

The last you could do by hand if you worked at it, but you're only chasing
about 5 clocks there, and require some fairly extensive ASM knowledge to
even do it.

OK. If I do:
float f;
...
f=(float)d;
if ( *(unsigned int *)&f > 0x40800000 ) goto bloopdone;

The inner loop comes out as:

fld ST(1)
; Line 22


fsub ST(0), ST(1)
fxch ST(3)

fmul QWORD PTR $T103


fxch ST(1)
fadd ST(0), ST(2)
fxch ST(3)

fxch ST(1)

fld ST(0)
fld ST(2)
fld ST(2)


fxch ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(4)

fxch ST(5)
fstp DWORD PTR _f$[esp-4]
mov edx, DWORD PTR _f$[esp-4]
fmulp ST(3), ST(0)
cmp edx, ecx
fmulp ST(1), ST(0)
jbe SHORT $L91

You can't just expect a compiler to use integer compares on
FP stuff - you've got to give it a little help. How many clocks is
that one? It looks somewhere around 20 (+/- 3) to me.
Not bad, huh? :P

like - VC doesn't seem to like doing that on it's own.

Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article
<6eetgs$5qu$1...@usenet45.supernews.com>...
>
> Ok, Brandon, what speedup would you expect if you were
> to write a complete application (say, a game) in 100%
> pure Alpha asm instead of using VC5/Alpha? And how
> much of that could you get from just doing the hotspots?
Despite my participation in these threads, I have never argued that one
should write an application entirely in ASM. :-) I have argued that ASM
is useful in the places where it's useful. My rule of thumb is I can do
80% of the optimization work in C, and the 20% remaining as scheduling
improvements in ASM. For problems that are amenable to scheduling
improvement. Memory caching, for instance, is often not one of them.

> >Finally, the ARM is a "predicated" architecture, most of the
> >instructions take an "if (X)" conditional as to whether they
> >should be executed or not.
>
> ARM is predicated, I believe.
>
> >At this point in our computational history, I doubt that
> >compiler writers have spent a lot of time figuring out the
> >potential scheduling benefits of such instructions.
>
> They f**king well should have.
> According to Intel's EPIC/IA64/Merced web pages, studies
> have shown that predication is a major win (reducing branches
> to 30%, IIRC).

Well they f**king well DIDN'T because it's the REAL WORLD we're programming
in, right? That's why ASM programmers get paid, we pick up the slack where
omniscient compiler theory and industrial practice meet. For a lot of
technologies, there's a 20 year horizon of transfer from academia to common
industrial practice. It certainly holds true in 3d graphics, all these hot
cheap 3d cards are using rendering techniques invented in the late 1970's.

> I'd have thought that using predication for most 'if-else' statements
> would be a remarkably obvious step to take in compiler
> design.

The number of registers available is still finite. If you have to consider
all the cases where registers could/couldn't be available, you have less
registers to play with. It is not the easy problem you make it out to be.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

required".

Well, with the Mandelbrot loop above, it got considerable help from my
prescheduling it. Perhaps you would like to give it:

do {
t = 2*x*y+i;
x = x*x-y*y+r;
y = t;
f = x*x+y*y;
} while( *(unsigned int *)&f < 0x40800000 );

And see how it does. Remember that a C progammer isn't neccesarily going to
know how to arrange loop the way I did, to make it faster for the compiler.
For that matter, they aren't going to see that float->integer +IEEE compare
trick either.

Sean T Barrett's profile photo
Sean T Barrett
unread,
Mar 18, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>You can't just expect a compiler to use integer compares on
>FP stuff - you've got to give it a little help.
Actually, you ought to expect them to do this
"right". If the integer sequence is always faster
(which is my understanding), then of course whoever
wrote the backend should have done this. The problem
is you can only use it for compares against a
constant which can be represented in single precision,
and that's a little more complicated.

But the point is every little optimization trick
in Agner Fog's doc _is_ available to compiler writers
too. And as long as they care about the performance
of their generated code, they will be trying to make
use of them. Hence the fact that the compiler
_does_ do the multiply-instead-of-a-divide.

>As for other algorithmic changes, I'll unroll the loop (in C) if you
>like - VC doesn't seem to like doing that on it's own.

Chris Hecker wrote two articles in Game Developer
about the generally poor quality of generated code,
especially on the x86.

There are two big issues that I'm aware of:

1) The stack model for x86 FPU code is a very
bad mismatch for modern compiler technology.
(There's been some research into it, but not
much. Register allocation is much better understood.)

2) Most compilers resist things like unrolling loops,
because they have no way of knowing which loops
are critical, and they don't want to bloat code.

There are two things to improve #2: compilers which
get feedback from profiling tools (there are a bunch
of these in academia, I don't know if there are
"commercial" unix compilers that do this yet), or
compilers that get hints from human beings. The
latter can either be done with commandline switches
(e.g. on a per-file basis, select how long a loop
is to allow it to be unrolled), or with embedded
instructions in the source code. At least one
language allowed the latter (I think a FORTRAN
variant, but I forget now), but it was generally
observed that human predictions tended to be
wrong, and tended to be poorly maintained.

Of course, I disagree with the latter--or rather,
that may be the results observed, but then the technology
was being misused. The point of the technology is
to allow a programmer who wants to improve bits of
his code to go in and say "this loop is critical"
and get it to unroll; that's less of a maintainence
headache than if it were recoded in assembly or
if a number of hacks (like those discussed above
and elsewhere in this thread) were applied.

Sean Barrett
maybe I should just give in and write my own compiler

> Actually, you ought to expect them to do this "right". If the integer

> whoever wrote the backend should have done this. The problem is you can
> only use it for compares against a constant which can be represented in

Right, you have to know how the float -> integer is going to round to know
that the indentical comparison occurs. Again, the C compiler is dead here,
since there is no way to communicate this through the C language. In fact,
the conversion demonstrated, I believe is actually slightly different
because of the rounding that will occur on conversion.

My recollecction of the rounding mode is that its probably equivalent to
something like: ( d < 3.9999999999995 )

> But the point is every little optimization trick in Agner Fog's doc _is_
> available to compiler writers too.

Except for the one above which could only be applied without compromise in
very rare circumstances.

This is equivalent to saying "The modern compiler technology is not up to

> (There's been some research into it, but not much. Register
> allocation is much better understood.)

I've done a little research into this myself, and strongly believe that
there is a way to map register methods to stack methods under the assumption
that FXCH is free (which is true on Intel CPUs.) I believe that Microsoft
knows/believe this as well. (My research is preliminary; I will put my
results up on my p5opt web page if I come up with anything interesting.)

> 2) Most compilers resist things like unrolling loops, because they have
> no way of knowing which loops are critical, and they don't want to
> bloat code.

Hmmm, then WATCOM is an exception. You can set its unrolling and inlining
thresholds. When did Chris Hecker write this?

> There are two things to improve #2: compilers which get feedback from
> profiling tools (there are a bunch of these in academia, I don't know if
> there are "commercial" unix compilers that do this yet),

Intel claims to have one that does this and plugs into the MSVC environment.
Its called "Proton". There are people inside of Intel who were very excited
about this technology, but I haven't heard much about it lately.

> [...] or compilers that get hints from human beings. The latter can

> either be done with commandline switches (e.g. on a per-file basis,
> select how long a loop is to allow it to be unrolled), or with embedded
> instructions in the source code.

Personally I would prefer:

#pragma unroll_thresh #quads

ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk said:
> Paul Hsieh wrote in message ...
> >ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk said:
> >[...] Compilers like WATCOM, have a completely different concept of

> >"inline" than the MSVC++ compiler.
>
> I'd like to see how any compiler could inline:
> ----- X.C -----

> unsigned int div3(unsigned int x) {
> return x/3;
> }
> ----- X.C -----
Well, its missing the "static" key word (and if by the bars you are implying
that that's the entire file, then sure, nothing can be done, but that isn't
realistic) however this will not stop the wiley WATCOM C/C++. Since it is
small enough it will implement this function *TWICE*; once as a static
within the current module (appearing inlined within the code) and once for
export.

> If I'd put a /3 in the middle of some random code then it wouldn't have the
> ret (and the other ops would be paired up with some other code). I figured
> that doing that would produce misleading results, so I put it in as a
> function.

Ok, lets forget this, its an entirely different issue.

> >everywhere.

Woops!

> [...] How about:


>
> do {
> t = 2*x*y+i;
> x = x*x-y*y+r;
> y = t;
> f = x*x+y*y;

> if( *(unsigned int *)&f > 0x40800000 ) goto bloopdone;

> And in asm:
> $L91:
+1 brt
u x > dec eax
u > test eax, eax
v > jle SHORT $L103
u x > fld ST(1)
u > fmul ST(0), ST(2) ; 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
+1 stall (fmul alu resource limit) ; 2 st(2)
u > fmul ST(0), ST(3) ; 3 st(2) 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
u > fsubr ST(0), ST(1) ; 1 ST(0) 2 st(2)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2) 2 st(0)
+1 stall (output dependency) ; 2 st(2) 3 st(0)
u > fmul QWORD PTR $T102 ; 3 st(2) 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
u > fadd ST(0), ST(4) ; 1 ST(0) 2 st(2)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2) 2 st(0)
+1 stall (output dependency) ; 2 st(2) 3 st(0)
u x > fsubr ST(0), ST(5) ; 3 st(2) 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
u > fstp ST(3) ; 2 st(2)
u x > fld ST(2) ; 3 st(2)
u > fmul ST(0), ST(3) ; 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
+2 > fst QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8] ; 3 st(2)
u > fmul QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8] ; 1 ST(0)
v > fxch ST(2) ; 1 st(2)
u x > fstp ST(1) ; 3 st(2)
u > fld QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8]
v > fxch ST(2)
u x > fadd ST(0), ST(1)
+2 > fstp DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8]
u > cmp DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8], ecx
v > jbe SHORT $L91

25 clocks. MSVC appears to have serious troubles scheduling this code
without assistance; 3 unnecessary stalls, besides the extra redundand
fstp(1). Notice that the fxch instruction is always to ST(2)? This causes a
dependency with results that are not yet computed, as I try to illustrate
with the comments. Although it successfully performed the subexpression
elimination (to work out x*x and y*y only once) this is clearly inferior
scheduling.

> Apparently that's a little slower. The RDTSC timings I've
> generated (on a P-II) are:
> 31 clock loop 3.0 million cycles
> 21 clock loop 1.7 million cycles
> Naive loop 1.85 million cycles
>
> That would imply about 10% slower - 23 clocks total, maybe?

The Pentium-II uses tricks to remove data dependencies, so that the two
"stall (output dependencies)" are likely to disappear. So in that sense,
like Brandon said, the P-II has a superior FPU. But as you can see, the
schedule assisted code still wins.

Of course precisely working out the exact clocks on a P-II is not an exact
science for me as of yet, especially with high latency, and out of order
happening at the same time.

> Not bad considering the naive mandelbrot loop... I guess the prescheduling
> helped a little, but not as much as you might have thought.

:o) All stemming from your assumption that you did well. Go dig up an old
Pentium and try it out on that. 25 clocks is *not* an awe inspiring result.



> >Remember that a C progammer isn't neccesarily going to
> >know how to arrange loop the way I did, to make it faster
> >for the compiler.
>

> See above - it didn't make a big difference.

25 clocks is closer to the originally unacceptable 31 clocks, than the
target of 16 clocks (I believe I misreported it in an eariler post as 15
clocks.)

> >For that matter, they aren't going to see that float->integer
> >+IEEE compare trick either.
>

> I'm not sure I would have, either - sign or zero checks, sure,

Again, the point is that a C programmer *can't* see this. Whereas an asm
programmer that cares about performance can look up FNSTSW to find it takes
6 clocks. That's the clue that should tell the ASM programmer "there must
be a better way".

> I might have thought of subtracting 4.0 and checking the
> sign, though. That would probably generate similar results.

Huh? I'd bet this is at least 1 clock slower, since you still have to do
the FCOM and FNSTSW, unless you get the FSUB for free.

Are you sure of this figure? If you ignore all the integer code, all the

means all the rest fits neatly into 2 clocks. This is possible, of course,

I think you've over-extrapolated. If there is one thing you've proven its
that P-II performance analysis is a lot different from Pentium performance
analysis. For FPU performance, I have only looked at Pentium optimization.
Even, if for some reason, you want to only look at P-II performance (where I
have yet to meet an authority) it is not known whether or not Damien or
Terje loops could not be significantly improved upon (they have minimum
"must execute" clocks of about 11.) But what I do know is that it will take
a human asm coder, not a compiler to determine this.

P-II and select Terje's loop in that case. (Thanks, this actually has some
relevance to me, for anyone following the MANDEL discussion on
comp.benchmarks; fortunately I have not released official results.)

vane...@blarg.net said:
> Paul Hsieh <q...@chromatic.com> wrote in article

> > I've done a little research into this myself, and strongly believe that
> > there is a way to map register methods to stack methods under the
> > assumption that FXCH is free (which is true on Intel CPUs.)
>
> No FXCH is not free. On a plain old Pentium you get *one* FXCH during a
> given CPU cycle, *if* you mind your P's and Q's properly with what came
> before and after it. The situation is better on Pentium II's but it is
> still a finite resource. It is entirely possible to write instruction
> sequences where you "would like" to have more FXCHs but they are simply not
> available to you.
I stand corrected.

> [...] Not to mention than the more FXCHs you have, the more you are
> watering down your code density.

Well, with the high latency of FPU operations, there isn't much to be lost
here anyways. Even the P6 can only issue one FPU operation per clock into
the FPU unit. On the P6 code density really applies more to non-FPU code.

ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk said:
> [...]

> >> 1) The stack model for x86 FPU code is a very bad
> >> mismatch for modern compiler technology.
> [...]
> I'd guess that you could just use standard register colouring
> to calculate the instructions required, then fxch to get the
> target register into st(0) before each op. I can't think of a case
> where that wouldn't work.
Well, that's all find and well, the problem is when you are in a loop. fxch
can pair off the rightly scheduled operands, but also starts permuting your
stack like crazy as a result. If your ordering isn't maintained in the
loop, well unless you have sufficient abelianness, isn't necessarily going
to work.

That code's wrong - there's no fallthrough or iteration
count. How about:

do {
t = 2*x*y+i;
x = x*x-y*y+r;
y = t;
f = x*x+y*y;

if( *(unsigned int *)&f > 0x40800000 ) goto bloopdone;
} while( (--c)>0 );

I trust that doesn't alter your pre-scheduling argument - the
if/goto is not much of a difference and certainly shouldn't
affect the maths...

And in asm:
$L91:
; Line 42


dec eax
test eax, eax

jle SHORT $L103
fld ST(1)
fmul ST(0), ST(2)
fxch ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(3)
fxch ST(2)
fsubr ST(0), ST(1)
fxch ST(2)
fmul QWORD PTR $T102
fxch ST(2)
fadd ST(0), ST(4)
fxch ST(2)
fsubr ST(0), ST(5)
fxch ST(2)
fstp ST(3)
fld ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(3)
fxch ST(2)


fst QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8]

fmul QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8]

fxch ST(2)
fstp ST(1)


fld QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8]

fxch ST(2)
fadd ST(0), ST(1)


fstp DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8]

cmp DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8], ecx

jbe SHORT $L91

Apparently that's a little slower. The RDTSC timings I've
generated (on a P-II) are:
31 clock loop 3.0 million cycles
21 clock loop 1.7 million cycles
Naive loop 1.85 million cycles

That would imply about 10% slower - 23 clocks total, maybe?

Not bad considering the naive mandelbrot loop...


I guess the prescheduling helped a little, but not as much
as you might have thought.

>know how to arrange loop the way I did, to make it faster

>+IEEE compare trick either.

but doing an integer compare for 'f>=4.0' is hardly obvious.

I might have thought of subtracting 4.0 and checking the
sign, though. That would probably generate similar results.

The pre-scheduled code with the compare moved (it *should*


make bugger all difference, but...):
looptop:

t = x*y;
x = x*x;
y = y*y;

d = x+y;
x = x-y+r;
y = 2*t+i;

f=(float)d;
if( *(unsigned int *)&f > 0x40800000 ) goto bloopdone;
if ( !(--c) ) goto bloopdone;
goto looptop;
bloopdone:;

Generates:
$looptop$91:
; Line 48
dec eax
je SHORT $L103
fld ST(0)
fld ST(2)
fxch ST(1)
fmul ST(0), ST(2)
fxch ST(1)
fmul ST(0), ST(3)


fxch ST(2)
fmul ST(0), ST(3)
fxch ST(1)

fst QWORD PTR _y$[esp+8]

fadd ST(0), ST(2)
fxch ST(2)


fsub QWORD PTR _y$[esp+8]

fxch ST(1)


fst QWORD PTR _t$[esp+8]

fmul QWORD PTR $T102
fxch ST(1)
fadd ST(0), ST(5)
fxch ST(2)


fst QWORD PTR _d$[esp+8]

fxch ST(1)
fsubr ST(0), ST(4)
fxch ST(1)

fstp DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8]

mov edx, DWORD PTR _f$[esp+8]
fxch ST(1)
fstp ST(2)
cmp edx, ecx
jbe SHORT $looptop$91

Which takes 1.43Mclocks (17-18 clocks / iteration?).

As far as I can tell, the P-II timings are:


31 clock loop 35.0 clocks
21 clock loop 19.8 clocks
Naive loop 21.0 clocks
Modified 21 16.25 clocks

Those ones are generated using RDTSC and c_brot(0,0)


and dividing the total time by 1000000 iterations
(since z=z^2+c doesn't terminate for 0+0i)

Does there need to be some extra setup code in Damien's
loop?
mov ecx,MI
fld st(0)
fld st(2)
seems to be missing on your web pagem (assuming the setup
is the same as Terje's loop). With those added, the code runs
in 14.8 clocks (over a million iterations).

That means I've got the compiler to within 10% of the fastest


version of the loop (in terms of actual performance).
I think that can be summed up in one word: Cool!

Paul Hsieh wrote in message ...
[...]
>> 1) The stack model for x86 FPU code is a very bad
>> mismatch for modern compiler technology.
[...]
>> (There's been some research into it, but not much.
>> Register allocation is much better understood.)
>
>I've done a little research into this myself, and strongly
>believe that there is a way to map register methods to
>stack methods under the assumption that FXCH is free
>(which is true on Intel CPUs.) I believe that Microsoft
>knows/believe this as well. (My research is preliminary;
>I will put my results up on my p5opt web page if I come
>up with anything interesting.)
I believe Intel consider this to be true as well - from their
Optimisation Manual:
"Most floating-point operations require that one operand
and the result use the top of stack. This makes each
instruction dependent on the previous instruction and
inhibits overlapping the instructions.
One obvious way to get around this is to imagine that we
have a flat floating-point register file available, rather than
a stack."[and then use fxch to achieve that]

I'd guess that you could just use standard register colouring
to calculate the instructions required, then fxch to get the
target register into st(0) before each op. I can't think of a case
where that wouldn't work.

---
Russ

Paul Hsieh <q...@chromatic.com> wrote in article
<MPG.f7a0a8cb...@nntp.chromatic.com>...

>
> I've done a little research into this myself, and strongly believe that
> there is a way to map register methods to stack methods under the
assumption
> that FXCH is free (which is true on Intel CPUs.)
No FXCH is not free. On a plain old Pentium you get *one* FXCH during a


given CPU cycle, *if* you mind your P's and Q's properly with what came
before and after it. The situation is better on Pentium II's but it is
still a finite resource. It is entirely possible to write instruction
sequences where you "would like" to have more FXCHs but they are simply not

available to you. Not to mention than the more FXCHs you have, the more


you are watering down your code density.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Sean T Barrett's profile photo
Sean T Barrett
unread,
Mar 19, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Paul Hsieh <q...@chromatic.com> wrote:
>Right, you have to know how the float -> integer is going to round to know
>that the indentical comparison occurs. Again, the C compiler is dead here,
>since there is no way to communicate this through the C language. In fact,
>the conversion demonstrated, I believe is actually slightly different
>because of the rounding that will occur on conversion.
>
>My recollecction of the rounding mode is that its probably equivalent to
>something like: ( d < 3.9999999999995 )
This may be contrary to the C spec, but there's
a general understanding that almost any optimization
of floating point will produce code with slightly
different behaviors, and most compilers have flags
to determine if they're allowed to "break" things.

For example, a simple loop adding up all the values
in an array can be sped up by using more than one
accumulator in parallel (a special variation on
unrolling), but this will produce slightly different
results. Under most conditions this is acceptable.

The rounding mode is a nightmare, and another
goofy artifact of x86. The whole IEEE rounding
mode business has _nothing_ to do with C, by
the way, as far as I know; it's just "implementation
defined".

Sean Barrett

Russ Williams

>27 clocks on a Pentium. Even worse than the above.

>serious hand holding (I.e., the loop I originally wrote.)

I guess the speedup was due to the P-II in that case.

Whatever - it's within 10% empirically which, considering
the nature of P6 optimisation, is a good way to compare.

>> Which takes 1.43Mclocks (17-18 clocks / iteration?).
>
>On a P-II?

Yup. That test is a Mandelbrot loop, -2.0 to 2.0 in steps of
0.1 for r and i: 1600 sample points.

>That would be truly remarkable. It would tell me that there
>are things about the P-II than I just don't know (perhaps
>the FST instruction is fully pipelined on the P-II or
>something like that.)

OK, according to VTune, there are 37 uops used in that loop
(in 13 decoder groups). The 37 uops are divided up as:
Integer 5
FXCH 11
Other 21 (15 instructions)

Even with parallel FP/int execution and totally free fxchs, the
P-II must execute >1 uop per clock for those FPU ops.

My guess: The fxchs and integer code are basically free,
fst/fstp have their second clocks pipelined (actually, this
is indicated by the diagram in the Optimisation Manual
(figure 5-2)). fmul/fsub when acting on memory generate
2 uops, but the load must get scheduled in parallel for
free. It appears that the P6 has 1 execution unit for FP
ops and FP ops share the load/store units with integer
code.

(Assuming VTune's static analysis is correct. I notice they
don't claim to be able to tell you clocks on a P6...)

>Are you sure of this figure? If you ignore all the integer code,
>all the fxch code, and assume no stalls (which is not a bad
>way to get a rough estimation)

That's what I was doing for my guesses before (but only
ignoring fxch and assuming no stalls).

>you get 15 "must happen" clocks (the previous was 16).
>Which means all the rest fits neatly into 2 clocks. This is
>possible, of course, but why this loop and not the loop
>above?

Who knows? The data dependencies look less in the
fast loop. The stores and loads fall into seperate
decoder groups, so perhaps the P6 has an inbuilt
assumption that everything that decodes together can
execute together (which might follow a logical progression
towards EPIC).

[...]


>> That means I've got the compiler to within 10% of the
>> fastest version of the loop (in terms of actual
>> performance). I think that can be summed up in
>> one word: Cool!
>
>I think you've over-extrapolated.

I've not extrapolated at all for that - the fast VC version is
9.7% slower than Damien's loop (11.3% slower than
Terje's), with 1000000 iterations. That's actual performance
as measured by RDTSC.

>If there is one thing you've proven its that P-II performance
>analysis is a lot different from Pentium performance
>analysis.

Definitely.

>For FPU performance, I have only looked at Pentium
>optimization. Even, if for some reason, you want to only
>look at P-II performance (where I have yet to meet an
>authority) it is not known whether or not Damien or Terje
>loops could not be significantly improved upon (they have
>minimum "must execute" clocks of about 11.) But what I
>do know is that it will take a human asm coder, not a
>compiler to determine this.

According to VTune, Terje's loop decodes in 7 clocks and
generates 1 uop for each instruction (except the store/load
of ftmp).

>> PPS - Terje's loop only takes 14.6 clocks...
>
>Actually, I was unaware, that Terje's loop was actually a little
>faster on the P-II (I thought they were identical.)

Fractions of a clock...

>Its not too surprising since fxch is probably a little more
>expensive on the P-II.

I thought fxch was totally free on the P-II since it never
needs to be executed.

>I will have to detect the P-II and select Terje's loop in
>that case.

It seems like Terje's loop is ~0.5 clocks faster. Perhaps
because the code is shorter?

trivial accept/reject tester for a 3d geometry transformation engine, it
uses *both* FPU and INT code, heavily. As will happen for any boolean test

use FPU-and-INT optimizations.

If any operation takes up space in the instruction cache, takes a pipeline
to execute, and takes X cycles to produce a result, then it is not "free."
It does have a cost, which you may or may not notice. Now, for any given
algorithm you can argue about how *cheap* it is... after all, a divide is
"free" if I can mask it with other work.

I also don't think there has to be a difference between a C programmer
and an assembly savvy programmer. Here in the ENDLESS THREAD, those
C-philes among us long ago acknowledged that a C programmer with no
knowledge of assembly is not likely to write blazing platform code.

time you're spending at each ASM line a good programmer will do just as
well in C as in ASM.

The question is not whether, but where to use ASM. Most ASM-only
advocates eventually admit that they use a macro language for large
scale projects. Well, C is basically a portable assembly macro
language.

gle...@cix.co.uk.notthisbityoumoron said:
> q...@chromatic.com (Paul Hsieh) wrote:
> > ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk said:
> > > I don't know about that - how many people would:
> > > a) Realise that you can do the remainder by dividing and
> > > rounding (OK, probably quite a lot, but the IDIV instruction
> > > does the modulus so on the face of it, it's slower). and
> > > b) Realise that it's possible to do integer division using
> > > a multiply (FP, sure, but the accuracy's a little different
> > > there...)
> >
> > I don't know but the asm programmer is going to have a much better
> > chance than the C programmer. The C programmer looks at (a%96) and is
> > likely to conclude that it is "indivisible". While the assembly savvy
> > programmer is likely to have a reference somewhere that shows the
> > clocks for DIV. The assembly savvy programmer is likely to say "30
> > clocks!! There has tobe a better way!" While the poor C programmer
> > has no way of even seeing those 30 clocks.
>
> If you want me to accept that in this case, the assembler programmer is
> going to spot the optimisation before the C programmer (fairly insulting
> IMO), would you accept that at a higher level, the C programmer is more
> likely to spot a superior algorithm when he hasn't got his head full of
> register usage and instruction paring?
Well, if you subscribe to the idea that being able to think about one thing
precludes a programmer from thinking of something else. The point is that a
high level algorithm isn't *hidden* from the assembly programmer, though
there may be more overhead involved in managing higher level design. Even
an assembly programmer, can ignore pairing and instruction clocks, and rely
on profiler feedback, and hence doesn't need to be "bogged down" by such
minutia. But either way, an assembly programmer doesn't need to be thinking
about all those things *simultaneously*.

The C programmer is *hidden* from the div penalty. There is no way for the
C programmer to definitively know one way or another how to deal with this
without involving some assembly. Even the hack Russ figured out to make
MSVC perform % with much greater performance *includes* a /, the expression
is essentially identical to a % and its not clear that the C compiler is
going to be able to optimize the / away. Without an assembly background how
is the C programmer going to know this?

> But does the assembler do it for you?, of course not, you have to code

I can't agree. C/C++ is supposed to be platform independent, and it pretty
much accomplishes this. The float->integer trick would actually *slow down*
other platforms. The trick only applies to the x86, and therefore is only
going to be well understood by people who know the assembly language
details.

Find me one single C programmer who doesn't know any x86 assembly that can
follow this discussion, and understand why the float->int trick and why
substituting x%y with x-((unsigned int)x/y)*y will improve performance on
an x86.

> [...] If I want to divide by three by using a multiply and a shift, it

> should be in the code, if only to help out any lesser C compiler that the
> code might be converted to later.

Perhaps you were not following this thread very carefully. In order to do
this, the C compiler must support the concept of a 32x32 -> 64 multiplier.
The only way to do that is either by supporting a 64 bit integer type (not a
necessity by ANSI C.)

> [...] If you want to compare two floating point numbers as integers there

> should be a macro or inline called AS_INT_COMPARE(a,b) or something. The
> key to efficient C is keeping a handle on what it's doing at an assembler
> level, i'd rather know what code an integer divide was going to produce
> than have it screw around in the background.

That's all find and well, if your C compiler or the ANSI C standard is going
to help you out. But this ignores the points I've made earlier about using
"unstable" optimizations that a C compiler simply cannot incorporate, or
accessing bizarre x86 functionality such as a 32x32 -> 64 bit multiplier.

Other examples include using XCHG, carry instructions, or partial registers.
You can find plenty of examples at:

http://www.pobox.com/~qed/asmexample.html
http://www.df.lth.se/~john_e/gems.html

Brandon Van Every wrote in message

>Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote in article

>>
>> I thought fxch was totally free on the P-II since it never
>> needs to be executed.
>
>If any operation takes up space in the instruction cache,
>takes a pipeline to execute, and takes X cycles to
>produce a result, then it is not "free."

But, the P6s are heavily into register renaming. The fxch
instruction would go from the decoder to the scheduler
and *not* into the execution units. All it does is trip the
renaming of a register - something the P6s do
automatically for integer code. Sure, it needs decoding,
but does it need an execution unit to 'execute'?

Wim Libaers's profile photo
Wim Libaers

to
>>Are there no assemblers/tools avaiable, that could
>>rearange your asm-code? :)
>
>Doubtful. Optimising assemblers were essentially killed
>by optimising compilers.


Borland TASM optimizes some individual instructions (i.e. replacing a
far call to a proc in the same code segment with a push cs, call near
sequence, some kinds of LEA,...), but you can turn the optimisations
off. Rearranging asm is almost impossible: the compiler in a high
level language knows what has to be returned, but apart from that, it
can do anything with the code generated. It does not, however,
understand the code. That would require VERY efficient AI. And this
makes it difficult to rearrange asm code: the assembler would need a
full understanding of the purpose to decide which instructions need to
be preserved and which ones don't. In a HLL, the guys who program the
compiler have figured it out for you.
Besides, they probably think you know what you're doing when you
decide to use asm.

(E-mail is anti-spammed)

It might be easier to write something in asm, rather than think about
how the compiler designers might have implemented an optimisation.
There is something worse than becoming platform-dependent (i.e. x86).
It's becoming compiler-dependent. If a new version of the compiler is
released, most of your knowledge might be obsolete.



Sean T Barrett's profile photo
Sean T Barrett
unread,
Mar 20, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>But, the P6s are heavily into register renaming. The fxch
>instruction would go from the decoder to the scheduler
>and *not* into the execution units. All it does is trip the
>renaming of a register - something the P6s do
>automatically for integer code. Sure, it needs decoding,
>but does it need an execution unit to 'execute'?
I personally gave up on learning how to optimize
for new x86 around the time the PPro was becoming
a reasonable platform. However, a few things
are worth stating, some fact, and some opinion
(and hopefully which is which is obvious):

- Out-of-order execution allows the _hardware_ to
schedule your code for you. in some cases it can
do better than you (e.g. if one instruction sometimes
takes 20 times as long [e.g. a cache miss]) because
it's dynamic. regardless, out-of-order execution
seems to make it a bit harder to beat compilers.

- Hardware designers like Intel make their design
decisions by emulating their hardware ideas on
real code. Decisions about cacheing, tradeoffs
in the number of execution units etc., are all
evaluated, not guessed at. The question is _what
code is used for this evaluation_. I suspect
they used a lot of compiler-generated code; it
doesn't exactly make sense to optimize for UV-paired
hand-coded Pentium-optimized assembly. Of course,
compilers never did crazy byte register things,
so Intel decided PRS was acceptable.

- The PPro's performance is so unpredictable that
an inner loop may behave differently on each
successive iteration, eventually "settling" into
a particular pattern (which may be more than one
iteration long). [This is part of why you can
get fractional cycle counts for a loop even with
no cache misses.]

- The PPro has a _lot_ of resources, any one of
which can be the "critical path" for a given bit
of code. There's execution units, instruction
decode, write buffers, completion buffer (or
whatever it's called), and a few more things as
well.

We're already familiar with the effective inability
to cycle count due to caches. Fortunately, caches
work in a modular fashion that make it straightforward
to count costs ignoring the cache, and introduce
penalties. However, on the PPro, all of the different
resources are working together, and so it's possible
to "stall" due to one resource being overloaded, and
then a few cycles later "stall" due to another one
being overloaded. (That's part of why you get the
different-performance-on-successive-iterations.)

None of this is to say an ASM programmer can't beat
a compiler. In fact, I wouldn't be that surprised
if an ASM programmer could still beat the compiler
by just following Pentium or K6 optimization rules.
But the _predictability_ seems to be gone.

Sean Barrett

Jason Shankel's profile photo
Jason Shankel

Only if you use compiler-dependent extensions. The easiest way to avoid
becoming compiler dependent is to keep your project current in 2 or 3
compilers.

ASM makes you not only platform dependent, but chip-generation
dependent. Optimization techniques differ greatly between the 486,
Pentium, PPro and Pentium II.

Paul Hsieh's profile photo

to

On Fri, 20 Mar 1998 06:04:04 GMT, buz...@world.std.com says...

> Russ Williams <ru...@algorithm.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> >But, the P6s are heavily into register renaming. The fxch instruction
> >would go from the decoder to the scheduler and *not* into the
> >execution units. All it does is trip the renaming of a register -
> >something the P6s do automatically for integer code. Sure, it needs
> >decoding, but does it need an execution unit to 'execute'?
>
> I personally gave up on learning how to optimize for new x86 around
> the time the PPro was becoming a reasonable platform.
Sorry to hear that. Even MSVC is stuck outputting brain dead RISC
instructions you know. Are you not aware that add [eax],ebx is a little
better than mov ecx,[eax] / add ecx,ebx / mov [eax],ecx ? Well, your
compiler isn't.

> [...] However, a few things are worth stating, some fact, and some

> opinion (and hopefully which is which is obvious):
>
> - Out-of-order execution allows the _hardware_ to schedule your code
> for you. in some cases it can do better than you (e.g. if one
> instruction sometimes takes 20 times as long [e.g. a cache miss])
> because it's dynamic. regardless, out-of-order execution seems to
> make it a bit harder to beat compilers.

Well, remember that compiler writers are not likely to know much more
about the PPro (or at least not likely to be able to deliver all its
performance advantages, given the highly complex execution model.) A
compiler isn't ordinarily going to "try a few possibilities out".
Something the asm coder still has a leg up on.

> - Hardware designers like Intel make their design decisions by
> emulating their hardware ideas on real code. Decisions about
> cacheing, tradeoffs in the number of execution units etc., are all
> evaluated, not guessed at. The question is _what code is used for
> this evaluation_. I suspect they used a lot of compiler-generated
> code; it doesn't exactly make sense to optimize for UV-paired hand-
> coded Pentium-optimized assembly. Of course, compilers never did
> crazy byte register things, so Intel decided PRS was acceptable.

WATCOM has always done "byte register things". The PPro is not too happy
about some of the Pentium optimized code that WATCOM produces. While
what you say is probably true, it doesn't mean that the CPU has "fast
booting of Windows" or "fast execution of MS-Word" instructions. The
core technology is still based on execution models not taken into account
by your compiler.

> - The PPro's performance is so unpredictable that an inner loop may
> behave differently on each successive iteration, eventually
> "settling" into a particular pattern (which may be more than one
> iteration long). [This is part of why you can get fractional cycle
> counts for a loop even with no cache misses.]

Its hard to predict *exactly* how a PPro performs. However, its usually
not hard to guess where a loop is limited and getting an approximation.
You still can applies rules of thumb, or heuristics, even today to
optimize for the PPro. I'm sure in the future we will have a much better
idea of how to optimize for the PPro.

> - The PPro has a _lot_ of resources, any one of which can be the
> "critical path" for a given bit of code. There's execution units,
> instruction decode, write buffers, completion buffer (or whatever
> it's called), and a few more things as well.

Yeah, but its usually easier than you think to figure out where you are
limited. Here's a hint: you are rarely, if ever, decode limited. (Only
in short loops, where the branch latency dominates the execution speed,
but in such situations unrolling is likely to remove this.)

> We're already familiar with the effective inability to cycle count due
> to caches.

This is another failing of C compilers. If you arrange your memory
declarations, just so, you can essentially "direct" their allocation
tendencies within the cache. I have not deeply investigated this, but
the theory is pretty straight forward. (The idea is to partion your
cache into your working set, and a tiny area for all your required "outer
loop" variables, then declare them in memory spaced out, so that their
relative address remainders modulo 16K end up as you've assigned them.)

> [...] Fortunately, caches work in a modular fashion that make it

> straightforward to count costs ignoring the cache, and introduce
> penalties. However, on the PPro, all of the different resources are
> working together, and so it's possible to "stall" due to one resource
> being overloaded, and then a few cycles later "stall" due to another
> one being overloaded. (That's part of why you get the different-
> performance-on-successive-iterations.)

Uh ... the real reason is that the re-order buffer may hold old micro-ops
from previous iterations. The PPro's re-order buffer in inferior to the
K6's in that its design makes it more conservative about issuing than it
needs to be.

> None of this is to say an ASM programmer can't beat a compiler. In
> fact, I wouldn't be that surprised if an ASM programmer could still
> beat the compiler by just following Pentium or K6 optimization rules.
> But the _predictability_ seems to be gone.

Well, I don't see how you can conclude this. Perfect predictability has
currently escaped us due to the complex nature of the PPro that is, as
usually, not sufficiently documented by Intel.

For example, the K6, is arguably as sophisticated as the PPro in terms of
performance and dynamic execution, with many if not more of the features
of the PPro. The rules for predicting the performance of the K6 are
fairly straight forward (it does require a lot of tracking, but its all
very well defined.)

With the PPro, we just have something of an anomoly.

--
Paul Hsieh
q...@pobox.com

Sean T Barrett <buz...@world.std.com> wrote in article
<Eq3su...@world.std.com>...

>
> None of this is to say an ASM programmer can't beat
> a compiler. In fact, I wouldn't be that surprised
> if an ASM programmer could still beat the compiler
> by just following Pentium or K6 optimization rules.
> But the _predictability_ seems to be gone.
Well, my experience has been that the predictability of optimization
improvements has never been terribly predictable to begin with. ;-p


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Brandon Van Every's profile photo
Brandon Van Every
unread,
Mar 20, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Sean T Barrett <buz...@world.std.com> wrote in article
<Eq1E7...@world.std.com>...

>
> But the point is every little optimization trick
> in Agner Fog's doc _is_ available to compiler writers
> too. And as long as they care about the performance

> of their generated code, they will be trying to make
> use of them. Hence the fact that the compiler
> _does_ do the multiply-instead-of-a-divide.
But you see compiler writers are not responsible for being 3d graphics
jocks or AI jocks or HW jocks etc. They don't have knowledge of all
possible domains of optimization, they only know some "general stuff." Why
should they know everything? Are they being paid to be on the cutting edge
of 3d graphics performance? No.

A great compiler is going to give you "pretty good" stuff. It is not going
to give you everything, and that's why people like myself can make a
living. This is the same issue as any other aspect of programming. You
can buy a lot of capabilities "off the shelf," and that's very convenient,
it enables you to get a lot of stuff done. But if you need something
that's not available off-the-shelf, then you have to buy some custom work.
The dynamic of this interchange is in fact why we have a SW industry.

I think it's no secret that compilers have mostly reacted to trends in OO
programming, not trends in high-performance programming.


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Sean T Barrett's profile photo
Sean T Barrett
unread,
Mar 20, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Brandon Van Every <vane...@blarg.net> wrote:
>But you see compiler writers are not responsible for being 3d graphics
>jocks or AI jocks or HW jocks etc. They don't have knowledge of all
>possible domains of optimization, they only know some "general stuff." Why
>should they know everything? Are they being paid to be on the cutting edge
>of 3d graphics performance? No.
Ever heard of "SPEC"?

Compiler-writers are at the front-line of the performance
wars. And SPEC uses relatively "real" applications, not just
dumb inner loops like most synthetic benchmarks.

If performance matters, and there is competition, then
compilers will optimize. What is the surprise here?

Compilers don't optimize as well as humans, is that
your claim? Yes, I've never disagreed; but your claim
is irrelevent; provide proof that in those domains ASM offers
more than a 10% speedup over the compiler writers--that is,
enough to matter. Before you complain that 10% matters, I'll
note that I already offered three lists of issuesa and
cases where ASM would be more useful than C, and got
0 mail messages and 0 followups addressing those lists.
Maybe it just got poorly distributed? Perhaps people
prefer to argue erratically rather than try to contribute
to constructive threads?

This is all a red-herring anyway.

I'll stand by this argument: One in N programmers needs
to learn asm, where N is probably 3-10. Recommending
that everyone learn it is arguing that _all_ game programmers
need to write super-polished high-performance code; but the
days of the 1-man game-coding shop have long since passed
us by.

Sean

Brandon Van Every
unread,

to

Sean T Barrett <buz...@world.std.com> wrote in article
<Eq53r...@world.std.com>...

> Brandon Van Every <vane...@blarg.net> wrote:
> >But you see compiler writers are not responsible for being 3d graphics
> >jocks or AI jocks or HW jocks etc. They don't have knowledge of all
> >possible domains of optimization, they only know some "general stuff."
Why
> >should they know everything? Are they being paid to be on the cutting
edge
> >of 3d graphics performance? No.
>
> Ever heard of "SPEC"?
>
> Compiler-writers are at the front-line of the performance
> wars. And SPEC uses relatively "real" applications, not just
> dumb inner loops like most synthetic benchmarks.
You just defeated the whole point of your argument when you inserted the
phrase "relatively." SPEC is a benchmark. Period.

Since we're taking turns making annoying comments that should be obvious to
the other party, how about this one: ever heard of Viewperf and GLPerf?
They're both SPEC benchmarks.
http://www.specbench.org/gpc/opc.static/index.html. Now, what do you have
to say to me regarding benchmarks, compilers, and the real world of 3d
graphics optimization?

> Compilers don't optimize as well as humans, is that
> your claim?

Within application-specific areas of optimization expertise, compilers will
*NEVER* optimize as well as humans. Compiler writers are not experts in
all fields, and technology is always a moving target. Heck, compiler
writers don't even start to do a good job with a given CPU architecture
until at least 2 years after that architecture is in common use, and that's
within their own field of expertise!

> Maybe it just got poorly distributed? Perhaps people
> prefer to argue erratically rather than try to contribute
> to constructive threads?

I don't know why you're badgering me in particular about any of this, I've
made numerous posts and stated my cases quite clearly. When I look at the
number of posts I've made in this thread, it's actually kinda disgusting
and I chide myself for it! Also don't confuse the various points that have
been made along the way. Just because this thread is titled as it is,
doesn't mean everyone's arguing about the same things.

> This is all a red-herring anyway.

Sez who, you? Think you know everything? At least others profit from the
discussion as they see many viewpoints.

> I'll stand by this argument: One in N programmers needs
> to learn asm, where N is probably 3-10. Recommending
> that everyone learn it is arguing that _all_ game programmers
> need to write super-polished high-performance code; but the
> days of the 1-man game-coding shop have long since passed
> us by.

And I'll stand by my argument that what you "need" to do is dependent on
what you like to do, how you'd like to advance your career, and how many
$$$$$ you'd like to get paid for it. If you're very good at ASM and you
enjoy it, and everyone else is pukeing and doesn't even want to deal with
it, well guess what supply-and-demand is going to make you worth a lot of
money. :-)


Cheers,
Brandon Van Every

Russ Williams's profile photo
Russ Williams
unread,
Mar 20, 1998, 9:00:00 AM
to

Brandon Van Every wrote in message
<01bd544c$5293af60$4883...@hammurabi.blarg.net>...

>Sean T Barrett <buz...@world.std.com> wrote in article
><Eq1E7...@world.std.com>...
>>
>> But the point is every little optimization trick
>> in Agner Fog's doc _is_ available to compiler writers
>> too. And as long as they care about the performance
>> of their generated code, they will be trying to make
>> use of them. Hence the fact that the compiler
>> _does_ do the multiply-instead-of-a-divide.
>
>But you see compiler writers are not responsible for being
>3d graphics jocks or AI jocks or HW jocks etc.
I don't see them doing 3d or AI, either.

>They don't have knowledge of all possible domains of
>optimization,

That's a completely different matter. They can't write
compilers that say "Fuck this! You couldn't write AI to
save your life! I'm rewriting it.", but I'd say they're well
up on the "turn some C into really fast asm" bit.

>they only know some "general stuff."

Like pairing, stalls and register allocation. That's most
of the 'grunt work' optimisation and, yes, it's pretty
general.

>Why should they know everything? Are they being paid
>to be on the cutting edge of 3d graphics performance?
>No.

Can you say 'straw man'?

>A great compiler is going to give you "pretty good" stuff.
>It is not going to give you everything, and that's why
>people like myself can make a living. This is the same
>issue as any other aspect of programming.

That will always be the case - things don't just program
themselves (at least not very well). Someone needs to
tell them what to produce. It used to be straight-to-the-CPU
assembly, now it's C/C++. In the future, it will probably be
automatic code generators ("insert a sprite routine here...")

>You can buy a lot of capabilities "off the shelf," and that's
>very convenient, it enables you to get a lot of stuff done.
>But if you need something that's not available off-the-shelf,
>then you have to buy some custom work. The dynamic of
>this interchange is in fact why we have a SW industry.

Yes. In what way does that relate to compiler performace
vs. asm?

>I think it's no secret that compilers have mostly reacted to
>trends in OO programming, not trends in high-performance
>programming.

They can get *very* close to optimal asm. Whether that's
by design, or a side-effect, it's the fact of it...

---
Russ

3mo • Edited •  3 months ago • Edited • Visible to anyone on or off LinkedIn

Follow

Today marks another major milestone for Clinivise...


We have officially brought on board our first employee!

Please join me in welcoming Jason Shankel as our new Director of Development!

Jason comes in at a whopping 30 years of industry experience and a deep technical background that will help shape the future of our platform.

Hailing from UCLA with degrees in Computer Science and Electrical Engineering, Jason has had an impressive career working at the intersection of software, AI, and gaming technology.

🎮 At Maxis/Electronic Arts, he played integral roles toward the development of SimCity and other genre-defining franchises.

🤖 At Roblox, he led engineering teams that built cutting-edge physics and AI systems for large-scale online gaming.

🏗️ Most recently, as CTO of Modumate, he was at the forefront of developing architectural modeling software using Unreal Engine technology.

I am honored to have someone with Jason’s talent, expertise, and passion for innovation join the team.

With Jason on board, Clinivise is taking a huge leap forward; not just in building an exceptional platform for hashtag#ABA supervision and document management, but also in expanding our R&D capabilities to develop and prototype state-of-the-art hashtag#AI systems that will improve clinical workflows and processes.

This is another step forward toward making Clinivise the leading platform for ABA supervision documentation and compliance.

Once again, join me in welcoming Jason to team Clinivise, and stay tuned for our upcoming open beta launch! 🚀

hashtag#Clinivise hashtag#RBT hashtag#BCBA hashtag#HealthTech hashtag#SoftwareDevelopment hashtag#Supervision hashtag#FieldworkTracking hashtag#Innovation hashtag#StartupGrowth hashtag#BehaviorAnalysis hashtag#AppliedBehaviorAnalysis
